<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a>.</div>

<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Sikhs" title="Category:Sikhs">Sikh Topics</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Sikhism_sidebar" title="Template:Sikhism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Sikhism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Sikhism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Sikhism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Sikhism</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/əm/: 'm' in 'rhythm'">əm</span></span>/</a></span></span>), also known as <b>Sikhi</b> (<a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>: <span lang="pa">ਸਿੱਖੀ</span> <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Sikkhī</i></span></i>, <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="pa-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Punjabi" title="Help:IPA/Punjabi">[ˈsɪkʰiː]</a></span>, from <span title="Punjabi-language text"><span lang="pa">ਸਿੱਖ</span></span>, <span title="Punjabi-language romanization"><i lang="pa-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikh" title="Sikh">Sikh</a></i></span>, 'disciple', 'seeker', or 'learner')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[i]</a></sup> or <b>Sikh Dharma</b>, is an <a href="/wiki/Indian_religions" title="Indian religions">Indian religion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[1]</a></sup> that originated in the <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a> region of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[ii]</a></sup> around the end of the 15th century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nesbitt2005_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nesbitt2005-5">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nirbhai_Singh_1990_1–3_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nirbhai_Singh_1990_1–3-6">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Opinderjit_Kaur_Takhar_2016_147_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Opinderjit_Kaur_Takhar_2016_147-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">[7]</a></sup> It is the most recently founded <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups" title="Major religious groups">major organized faith</a> and stands at fifth-largest worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup> with about 25–30 million adherents (known as <a href="/wiki/Sikhs" title="Sikhs">Sikhs</a>) as of the early 21st century<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sikhism developed from the spiritual teachings of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> (1469–1539), the faith's first <a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> and the nine <a href="/wiki/Sikh_gurus" title="Sikh gurus">Sikh gurus</a> who succeeded him. The tenth guru, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Gobind Singh</a> (1666–1708), named the Sikh scripture <i><a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</a></i> as his successor, bringing to a close the line of human gurus and establishing the scripture as the 11th and last eternally living guru, a religious spiritual/life guide for Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_McLeod_2014_page_17_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-WH_McLeod_2014_page_17-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_James_2011_pages_241-242_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-William_James_2011_pages_241-242-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-granthfinalguru_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-granthfinalguru-18">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> taught that living an "active, creative, and practical life" of "truthfulness, fidelity, self-control and purity" is above metaphysical truth, and that the ideal man "establishes union with God, knows His Will, and carries out that Will".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marwha_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marwha-19">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a>, the sixth Sikh Guru (1606–1644), established the concept of mutual co-existence of the <a href="/wiki/Miri_piri" title="Miri piri"><i>miri</i></a> ('political'/'temporal') and <i>piri</i> ('spiritual') realms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marty-20">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sikh scripture opens with the <i><a href="/wiki/Mul_Mantar" title="Mul Mantar">Mul Mantar</a></i> (<span title="Punjabi-language text"><span lang="pa">ਮੂਲ ਮੰਤਰ</span></span>), fundamental prayer about <i><a href="/wiki/Ik_Onkar" title="Ik Onkar">ik onkar</a></i> (<span title="Punjabi-language text"><span lang="pa">ੴ</span></span>, '<a href="/wiki/God_in_Sikhism" title="God in Sikhism">One God</a>').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pashauramulmantar_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-pashauramulmantar-21">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singhaikonkar_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-singhaikonkar-22">[20]</a></sup> The core beliefs of Sikhism, articulated in the <i><a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</a></i>, include <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simran" title="Simran">meditation</a> in the name of the <a href="/wiki/Ik_Onkar" title="Ik Onkar">one creator</a>; <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divine</a> unity and equality of all humankind; engaging in <i>seva</i> ('<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selfless_service" title="Selfless service">selfless service</a>'); striving for justice for the <a href="/wiki/Sarbat_da_bhala" title="Sarbat da bhala">benefit and prosperity of all</a>; and honest conduct and livelihood while living a householder's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalsi_Chelsea_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalsi_Chelsea-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_Sambhi_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cole_Sambhi-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teece_2004_4_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Teece_2004_4-25">[23]</a></sup> Following this standard, Sikhism rejects claims that any particular religious tradition has a monopoly on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_truth" title="Absolute truth">Absolute Truth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[iii]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup> Sikhism emphasizes <i><a href="/wiki/Simran" title="Simran">simran</a></i> (<span title="Punjabi-language text"><span lang="pa">ਸਿਮਰਨ</span></span>, meditation and remembrance of the teachings of Gurus),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup> which can be expressed musically through <i><a href="/wiki/Kirtan" title="Kirtan">kirtan</a></i>, or internally through <a href="/wiki/Naam_Japo" title="Naam Japo"><i>naam japna</i></a> ('meditation on His name') as a means to feel God's presence. It teaches followers to transform the "<a href="/wiki/Five_Thieves" title="Five Thieves">Five Thieves</a>" (i.e. lust, rage, greed, attachment, and ego).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kamala_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kamala-29">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The religion developed and evolved in times of <a href="/wiki/Religious_persecution#Persecutions_of_Sikhs" title="Religious persecution">religious persecution</a>, gaining converts from both <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2008_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2008-30">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal rulers</a> of India tortured and executed two of the Sikh gurus—<a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> (1563–1605) and <a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Guru Tegh Bahadur</a> (1621–1675)—after <a href="/wiki/Forced_conversion#Islam" title="Forced conversion">they refused to convert to Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup>
The persecution of Sikhs triggered the founding of the <i><a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a></i> by Guru Gobind Singh in 1699 as an order to protect the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_of_conscience" title="Freedom of conscience">freedom of conscience</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">religion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gandhi_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gandhi-36">[33]</a></sup> with members expressing the qualities of a <i><a href="/wiki/Sant_Sipahi" title="Sant Sipahi">Sant-Sipāhī</a></i> ('saint-soldier').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chanchreek_2007_142_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chanchreek_2007_142-37">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dugga_2001_33_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dugga_2001_33-38">[35]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The majority of Sikh scriptures were originally written in the alphabet of <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi"><i>Gurmukhī</i></a>, a script standardised by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Angad" title="Guru Angad">Guru Angad</a> out of <a href="/wiki/La%E1%B9%87%E1%B8%8D%C4%81_scripts" title="Laṇḍā scripts">Laṇḍā scripts</a> historically used in present-day Pakistan and <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShackleMandair2013pxxi_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShackleMandair2013pxxi-40">[37]</a></sup> Adherents of Sikhism are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Sikhs" title="Sikhs">Sikhs</a></i>, meaning 'students' or 'disciples' of the Guru. The <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> word <i>Sikhism</i> derives from the Punjabi verb <i>Sikhi</i>, which connotes the "temporal path of learning" and is rooted in the word <span title="Punjabi-language romanization"><i lang="pa-Latn">sikhana</i></span> ('to learn').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandair2013p12_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair2013p12-41">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_and_teachings">Philosophy and teachings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Philosophy and teachings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ik_Onkar" title="Ik Onkar">Ik Onkar</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:33%; ; padding:8px;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<div class="poem">
<p>The Definition of Sikh is any human being who faithfully believes in:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[40]</a></sup><br/>
I. One Immortal Being,<br/>
II. Ten Gurus, from Guru Nanak Sahib to Guru Gobind Singh Sahib,<br/>
III. The Guru Granth Sahib,<br/>
IV. The utterances and teachings of the ten Gurus and,<br/>
V. The baptism bequeathed by the tenth Guru, and who does not owe allegiance to any other religion, is a Sikh.
</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
</div>
<p>Sikhism is classified as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_religion" title="Indian religion">Indian religion</a> along with <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reichberg2014p672_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reichberg2014p672-44">[iv]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[v]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The basis of Sikhism lies in the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> and his successors.  Sikh ethics emphasize the congruence between spiritual development and everyday moral conduct. Its founder Guru Nanak summarized this perspective as: "Truth is the highest virtue, but higher still is truthful living."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 234">: 234 </span></sup> Sikhism lays emphasis on <i>Ėk nūr te sab jag upjiā</i>, 'From the one light, the entire universe welled up.'<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teachings">Teachings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Teachings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sikhism is a monotheistic religion with pantheistic elements, advocating the belief in One Universal God signified by the term <a href="/wiki/Ik_Onkar" title="Ik Onkar">Ik Onkar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup> In Sikhism, the overall concept of God is <i><a href="/wiki/Waheguru" title="Waheguru">Waheguru</a></i> ('wondrous Teacher') considered to be <i><a href="/wiki/Nirankar" title="Nirankar">nirankar</a></i> ('shapeless'), <a href="/wiki/Akal_(Sikh_term)" title="Akal (Sikh term)"><i>akal</i></a> ('timeless'), <i>karta purakh</i> ('the creator'), and <i>agam agochar</i> ('incomprehensible and invisible').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a literal sense, God has no gender in Sikhism, though metaphorically, God is presented as masculine and God's power as feminine. For example, God is repeatedly referred to by the name <i>akaal purkh</i> ('beyond time and space') and <i><a href="/wiki/Nirankar" title="Nirankar">nirankar</a></i> ('without form') by the tenth guru Guru Gobind Singh Ji, but he also refers to God as his father, and God's creative power as his mother. Similarly, another example is that the scripture and eternal guru, the Guru Granth Sahib says that all humans are soul-brides who long to unite with their husband Lord.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[46]</a></sup> In addition, the gurus also wrote in the Guru Granth Sahib that there are many worlds on which the transcendental God has created life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sikh scripture begins with God as <i><a href="/wiki/Ik_Onkar" title="Ik Onkar">ik onkar</a></i> (<span title="Punjabi-language text"><span lang="pa">ੴ</span></span>), the 'formless one',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singhaikonkar_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-singhaikonkar-22">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 227">: 227 </span></sup> understood in the Sikh tradition as monotheistic unity of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[48]</a></sup> <i>Ik onkar</i> (sometimes capitalized) is more loosely rendered 'the one supreme reality', 'the one creator', 'the all-pervading <a href="/wiki/Para_Brahman" title="Para Brahman">spirit</a>', and other ways of expressing a diffused but unified and singular sense of God and creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The traditional <i><a href="/wiki/Mul_Mantar" title="Mul Mantar">Mul Mantar</a></i> goes from <i>ik onkar</i> until <i>Nanak hosee bhee sach</i> Guru Nanak (the first guru of sikhs) is living forever. The existence of guru is eternal. Sach means right, true, real. It means Guru Nanak is  real from ages and will remain true.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title='Which means what? And what is "goes from" supposed to convey here? Why does it matter? What is its pertinence to this section? (November 2020)'>clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The opening line of the <i>Guru Granth Sahib</i> and each subsequent <i><a href="/wiki/Raga" title="Raga">raga</a></i>, mentions <i>ik onkar</i>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pashauramulmantar2_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-pashauramulmantar2-55">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1109925960">.mw-parser-output .verse_translation .translated{padding-left:2em}@media only screen and (max-width:43.75em){.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small td{display:block;padding-left:0}.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small .translated{padding-left:0}}</style>
<table class="verse_translation" role="presentation" style="margin-left:1em;">
<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><div class="poem" lang="pa" style="font-style:roman;text-align:left">
<p>ੴ ਸਤਿ ਨਾਮੁ ਕਰਤਾ ਪੁਰਖੁ ਨਿਰਭਉ ਨਿਰਵੈਰੁ ਅਕਾਲ ਮੂਰਤਿ ਅਜੂਨੀ ਸੈਭੰ ਗੁਰ ਪ੍ਰਸਾਦਿ॥<br/><br/>
<i>ikk ōankār sat<small>(i)</small>-nām<small>(u)</small> karatā purakh<small>(u)</small> nirabha'u niravair<small>(u)</small> akāl<small>(a)</small> mūrat<small>(i)</small> ajūnī saibhan gur<small>(a)</small> prasād<small>(i)</small>.</i>
</p>
</div>
</td>
<td class="translated"><div class="poem">
<p>"There is one supreme being, the eternal reality, the creator, without fear and devoid of enmity, immortal, never incarnated, self-existent, known by grace through the true Guru."
</p>
</div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top; font-size:90%">
<td>—<i><a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</a></i> (17th c.), p. 1
</td>
<td style="padding-left: 2em;">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldly_Illusion">Worldly Illusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Worldly Illusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Akalees_Emily_Eden_PDL.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Akalees_Emily_Eden_PDL.jpg/220px-Akalees_Emily_Eden_PDL.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Akalees_Emily_Eden_PDL.jpg/330px-Akalees_Emily_Eden_PDL.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Akalees_Emily_Eden_PDL.jpg/440px-Akalees_Emily_Eden_PDL.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Akalees_Emily_Eden_PDL.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sketch made in 1844 by <a href="/wiki/Emily_Eden" title="Emily Eden">Emily Eden</a> of the "Akalees or Immortals". Digitized by the <a href="/wiki/Panjab_Digital_Library" title="Panjab Digital Library">Panjab Digital Library</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_(illusion)" title="Maya (illusion)">Māyā</a></i>, defined as a temporary illusion or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unreality" title="Unreality">unreality</a>", is one of the core deviations from the pursuit of God and salvation: where worldly attractions give only illusory temporary satisfaction and pain that distracts from the process of the devotion of God. However, Nanak emphasised māyā as not a reference to the unreality of the world, but of its values. In Sikhism, the influences of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahankar" title="Ahankar">ego</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krodh" title="Krodh">anger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lobh" title="Lobh">greed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moh" title="Moh">attachment</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kam" title="Kam">lust</a>, known as the <i>pānj chor</i> ('<a href="/wiki/Five_Thieves" title="Five Thieves">five thieves</a>'), are believed to be particularly distracting and hurtful. Sikhs believe the world is currently in a state of <i><a href="/wiki/Kali_Yuga" title="Kali Yuga">kali yuga</a></i> ('age of darkness') because the world is led astray by the love of and attachment to <i>māyā</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[51]</a></sup> The fate of people vulnerable to the five thieves, is separation from God, and the situation may be remedied only after intensive and relentless devotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeless_Truth">Timeless Truth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Timeless Truth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="469" data-file-width="703" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg/220px-Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg/330px-Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg/440px-Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nihang_singh" title="Nihang singh">Akali-Nihang Sikh Warrior</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harmandir_Sahib" title="Harmandir Sahib">Harmandir Sahib</a>, also called the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple" title="Golden Temple">Golden Temple</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to Guru Nanak, the supreme purpose of human life is to reconnect with <a href="/wiki/Akal_(Sikh_term)" title="Akal (Sikh term)"><i>Akal</i></a> ('The Timeless One'). However, <a href="/wiki/Egotism" title="Egotism">egotism</a> is the biggest barrier in making this connection. Using the Guru's teaching remembrance of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C4%81m_Jap%C5%8D" title="Nām Japō">nām</a></i> (the divine Name of the Lord)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pruthi_2004_204_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pruthi_2004_204-58">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NaamSimran_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-NaamSimran-59">[54]</a></sup> leads to the end of egotism. Guru Nanak designated the word <i>Guru</i> ('teacher')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup> to mean the voice of "the spirit": the source of knowledge and the guide to salvation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p254_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-p254-61">[56]</a></sup> As <i>ik onkar</i> is <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">universally immanent</a>, <i>Guru</i> is indistinguishable from <i>Akal</i> and are one and the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singh2013_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-singh2013-62">[57]</a></sup> One connects with <i>Guru</i> only with accumulation of selfless search of truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dhillon1999_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-dhillon1999-63">[58]</a></sup> Ultimately the seeker realises that it is the consciousness within the body which is the seeker/follower of the Word that is the true <i>Guru</i>. The human body is just a means to achieve the reunion with Truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singh2013_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-singh2013-62">[57]</a></sup> Once truth starts to shine in a person's heart, the essence of current and past holy books of all religions is understood by the person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dhillon2004_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-dhillon2004-64">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberation">Liberation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Liberation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Guru Nanak's teachings are founded not on a final destination of heaven or hell, but on a spiritual union with the <i>Akal</i>, which results in <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Jivanmukta" title="Jivanmukta">jivanmukti</a></i> ('enlightenment/liberation within one's lifetime'),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[60]</a></sup> a concept also found in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grewal_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grewal-66">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a> makes it clear that human birth is obtained with great fortune, therefore one needs to be able to make the most of this life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chahal2011_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-chahal2011-67">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sikhs accept <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a> concepts found in <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, but do not necessarily infer a <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> <a href="/wiki/Soteriology" title="Soteriology">soteriology</a> akin to those found in those other religions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Make sense of that in plain English; 99.999% of our readers do not have philosophy degrees. (November 2020)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chahal2011_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-chahal2011-67">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[64]</a></sup> However, in Sikhism, both karma and liberation "is modified by the concept of God's grace" (<i>nadar, mehar, kirpa, karam</i>, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grewal_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grewal-66">[61]</a></sup> Guru Nanak states that "the body takes birth because of karma, but salvation is attained through grace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup> To get closer to God, Sikhs: avoid the evils of <i>maya</i>; keep the everlasting truth in mind; practice <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shabad_(hymn)" title="Shabad (hymn)">shabad</a> <a href="/wiki/Kirtan" title="Kirtan">kirtan</a></i> (musical recitation of hymns); <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meditate" title="Meditate">meditate</a> on <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C4%81m_Jap%C5%8D" title="Nām Japō">naam</a></i>; and serve humanity. Sikhs believe that being in the company of the <i><a href="/wiki/Satsang" title="Satsang">satsang</a></i> (association with <i>sat</i>, 'true', people) or <i>sadh <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sangat_(term)" title="Sangat (term)">sangat</a></i> is one of the key ways to achieve liberation from the cycles of reincarnation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Power_and_Devotion_.28Miri_and_Piri.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_and_Devotion_(Miri_and_Piri)">Power and Devotion (Miri and Piri)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Power and Devotion (Miri and Piri)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Miri-Piri</b> is a doctrine that has been practiced in Sikh religion since the seventeenth century. The doctrine of the "Mir" (social and political aspects of life) and the "Pir" (guides to spiritual aspect of life) was revealed by the first Guru of Sikhism, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a>, but propounded by the sixth Guru of Sikhism, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a>, on June 12, 1606. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jas_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-jas-72">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grove_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-grove-73">[68]</a></sup> After the martyrdom of his father, Guru Hargobind was elevated to the Guruship and fulfilled the prophecy that was given by the primal figure of Sikh, <a href="/wiki/Baba_Buddha" title="Baba Buddha">Baba Buddha</a>, that the guru will possess spiritual and temporal power. <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a> introduced the two swords of Miri and Piri symbolizing both worldly (social and political) and spiritual authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jas_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-jas-72">[67]</a></sup> The two <a href="/wiki/Kirpan" title="Kirpan">kirpan</a> of Miri and Piri are tied together with a <a href="/wiki/Khanda_(Sikh_symbol)" title="Khanda (Sikh symbol)">khanda</a> in center, so the combination of both is considered supreme,  Where action informed or arising out of the spiritual heart completes one's purpose and meaning in the world of action:  <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spirituality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jas_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-jas-72">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a>, the first Sikh Guru and the founder of Sikhism, was a <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Bhakti</a> saint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-richard_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-richard-76">[71]</a></sup> He taught that the most important form of worship is <i>Bhakti</i> (devotion to <a href="/wiki/Waheguru" title="Waheguru">Waheguru</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayled2002_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mayled2002-77">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a>, in the <i><a href="/wiki/Sukhmani_Sahib" title="Sukhmani Sahib">Sukhmani Sahib</a></i>, recommended the true religion is one of loving devotion to God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</a></i> includes suggestions on how a Sikh should perform constant Bhakti.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayled2002_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mayled2002-77">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sggs305306_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-sggs305306-80">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jhutti-Johal2011_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jhutti-Johal2011-81">[76]</a></sup> Some scholars call Sikhism a Bhakti sect of Indian traditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davidlorenzen_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-davidlorenzen-83">[78]</a></sup> adding that it emphasises "<i>nirguni Bhakti</i>," i.e. loving devotion to a divine without qualities or physical form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davidlorenzen_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-davidlorenzen-83">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1–3">: 1–3 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hardip_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-hardip-84">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[80]</a></sup> While Western scholarship generally places Sikhism as arising primarily within a Hindu Bhakti movement milieu while recognizing some <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi Islamic</a> influences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-87">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 3, 42–3">: 3, 42–3 </span></sup> some Indian Sikh scholars disagree and state that Sikhism transcended the environment it emerged from. The basis of the latter analysis is that Bhakti traditions did not clearly disassociate from Vedic texts and their cosmologies and metaphysical worldview, while the Sikh tradition clearly did disassociate from the Vedic tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singha_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singha-88">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some Sikh sects outside the <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab</a> region of India, such as those found in <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, practice <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aarti" title="Aarti">aarti</a></i> (the ceremonial use of lamps) during Bhakti observances in a Sikh <a href="/wiki/Gurdwara" title="Gurdwara">gurdwara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[85]</a></sup> But, most Sikh gurdwaras forbid <i>aarti</i> during their Bhakti practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-87">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 201">: 201 </span></sup>
</p><p>While emphasizing Bhakti, the Sikh gurus also taught that the spiritual life and secular householder life are intertwined, and not separate. This logically follows from the panentheistic nature of Sikh philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kamala1_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kamala1-91">[86]</a></sup> In Sikh worldview, the everyday world is part of the Infinite Reality, increased spiritual awareness leads to increased and vibrant participation in the everyday world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nikky1_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nikky1-92">[87]</a></sup> Guru Nanak described living an "active, creative, and practical life" of "truthfulness, fidelity, self-control and purity" as being higher than the <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marwha1_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marwha1-93">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 6th Sikh Guru, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a>, after <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a>'s martyrdom, faced with oppression by the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>, affirmed the philosophy that the political/temporal (<i>Miri</i>) and spiritual (<i>Piri</i>) realms are mutually coexistent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty1_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marty1-94">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gandhi1_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gandhi1-95">[90]</a></sup> According to the 9th Sikh Guru, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tegh_Bahadur" title="Tegh Bahadur">Tegh Bahadur</a>, the ideal Sikh should have both <i><a href="/wiki/Shakti" title="Shakti">Shakti</a></i> (power that resides in the temporal), and <i><a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Bhakti</a></i> (spiritual meditative qualities). This was developed into the concept of the "saint soldier" by the 10th Sikh Guru, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gobind_Singh" title="Gobind Singh">Gobind Singh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gandhi1_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gandhi1-95">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of humanity as elaborated by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> refines and negates the "monotheistic concept of self/God", and "monotheism becomes almost redundant in the movement and crossings of love."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandair1_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair1-96">[91]</a></sup> The human's goal, taught the Sikh gurus, is to end all dualities of "self and other, I and not-I", attain the "attendant balance of separation-fusion, self-other, action-inaction, attachment-detachment, in the course of daily life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandair1_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair1-96">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singing_and_Music">Singing and Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Singing and Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sikhs refer to the hymns of the gurus as <i><a href="/wiki/Gurbani" title="Gurbani">Gurbani</a></i> ('Guru's word'). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shabad_(hymn)" title="Shabad (hymn)">Shabad</a> <a href="/wiki/Kirtan" title="Kirtan">Kirtan</a> is the singing of Gurbani. The entire verses of Guru Granth Sahib are written in a form of poetry and rhyme to be recited in thirty-one Ragas of the Classical Indian Music as specified. However, the exponents of these are rarely to be found amongst the Sikhs who are conversant with all the Ragas in the Guru Granth Sahib. Guru Nanak started the Shabad Kirtan tradition and taught that listening to kirtan is a powerful way to achieve tranquility while meditating, and singing of the glories of the Supreme Timeless One (God) with devotion is the most effective way to come in communion with the Supreme Timeless One.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[92]</a></sup> The three morning prayers for Sikhs consist of Japji Sahib, Jaap Sahib, and Tav-Prasad Savaiye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diaspora_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-diaspora-98">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Baptised Sikhs</a> (Amritdharis) rise early and meditate, then recite all the <a href="/wiki/Five_Banis" title="Five Banis">Five Banis</a> of Nitnem, before breakfast. <a href="/wiki/Five_Banis" title="Five Banis">Five Banis</a> consists of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jap_Ji_Sahib" title="Jap Ji Sahib">Jap Ji Sahib</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jaap_Sahib" title="Jaap Sahib">Jaap Sahib</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tav-Prasad_Savaiye" title="Tav-Prasad Savaiye">Tav-Prasad Savaiye</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaupai_Sahib" title="Chaupai Sahib">Chaupai Sahib</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anand_Sahib" title="Anand Sahib">Anand Sahib</a> and recitation of the banis <a href="/wiki/Paath" title="Paath">paath</a> is followed by <a href="/wiki/Ard%C4%81s" title="Ardās">Ardās</a> in which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarbat_da_Bhala" title="Sarbat da Bhala">Sarbat da Bhala</a> principle is taught by Gurus which literally means blessings for everyone, blessings to humankind in good faith without discrimination.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Remembrance_of_the_Divine_Name">Remembrance of the Divine Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Remembrance of the Divine Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A key practice by Sikhs is remembrance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NaamSimran_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-NaamSimran-59">[54]</a></sup> of the <i>Naam</i> (divine name) Waheguru.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pruthi_2004_204_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pruthi_2004_204-58">[53]</a></sup> This contemplation is done through <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C4%81m_Jap%C5%8D" title="Nām Japō">Nām Japna</a></i> (repetition of the divine name) or <i><a href="/wiki/Simran" title="Simran">Naam Simran</a></i> (remembrance of the divine Name through recitation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NaamSimran_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-NaamSimran-59">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup> The verbal repetition of the name of God or a sacred syllable has been an ancient established practice in religious traditions in India, however, Sikhism developed <i>Naam-simran</i> as an important Bhakti practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dhillon1988_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dhillon1988-100">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[97]</a></sup> Guru Nanak's ideal is the total exposure of one's being to the divine Name and a total conforming to <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a> or the "Divine Order". Nanak described the result of the disciplined application of <i>nām simraṇ</i> as a "growing towards and into God" through a gradual process of five stages. The last of these is <i><a href="/wiki/Sach_Khand" title="Sach Khand">Sach Khaṇḍ</a></i> (<i>The Realm of Truth</i>) –  the final union of the spirit with God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p254_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-p254-61">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Service_and_Action">Service and Action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Service and Action">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seva_(Indian_religions)#Seva_in_Sikhism" title="Seva (Indian religions)">Seva (Indian religions) § Seva in Sikhism</a></div>
<p>The Sikh gurus taught that by constantly remembering the divine name (<i><a href="/wiki/Simran" title="Simran">naam simran</a></i>) and through selfless service (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seva_(Indian_religions)" title="Seva (Indian religions)">sēvā</a></i>) the devotee overcomes egotism (<i><a href="/wiki/Haumai" title="Haumai">Haumai</a></i>). This, it states, is the primary root of five evil impulses and the cycle of birth and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Service in Sikhism takes three forms: <i>Tan</i> (physical service, i.e. labor), <i>Man</i> (mental service, such as dedicating your heart for service of others), and <i>Dhan</i> (material service, including financial support).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[100]</a></sup> Sikhism stresses <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kirat_kar%C5%8D" title="Kirat karō">kirat karō</a></i>: that is "honest work". Sikh teachings also stress the concept of sharing, or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Va%E1%B9%87%E1%B8%8D_chakk%C5%8D" title="Vaṇḍ chakkō">vaṇḍ chakkō</a></i>, giving to the needy for the benefit of the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Justice_and_Equality">Justice and Equality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Justice and Equality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sikhism regards God as the true king, the king of all kings, the one who dispenses justice through the law of <i><a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a></i>, a retributive model and divine grace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Justice_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Justice-107">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chanchreek_2007_142_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chanchreek_2007_142-37">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dugga_2001_33_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dugga_2001_33-38">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term for justice in the Sikh tradition is  Niāyā   <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Justice_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Justice-107">[102]</a></sup> It is related to the term <i>dharam</i> which in Sikhism connotes 'moral order' and righteousness (derived from but become distinct from the etymologically related Hindu concept of <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Justice_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-Justice-107">[102]</a></sup> According to the Tenth Sikh Guru, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a>, states Pashaura Singh (a professor of Sikh studies), "one must first try all the peaceful means of negotiation in the pursuit of justice" and if these fail then it is legitimate to "draw the sword in defense of righteousness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[103]</a></sup> Sikhism considers "an attack on dharam is an attack on justice, on righteousness, and on the moral order generally" and the dharam "must be defended at all costs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mcleod_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mcleod-109">[104]</a></sup> The divine name is its antidote for pain and vices. Forgiveness is taught as a virtue in Sikhism, yet it also teaches its faithful to shun those with evil intentions and to pick up the sword to fight injustice and religious persecution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sikhism does not differentiate religious obligations by sex. God in Sikhism has no sex, and the Sikh scripture does not discriminate against women, nor bar them from any roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2009p70_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2009p70-111">[106]</a></sup> Women in Sikhism have been in positions of leadership, including leading in wars and issuing orders or <a href="/wiki/Hukamnama" title="Hukamnama">hukamnamas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2009p70_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2009p70-111">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ten_Gurus_and_Authority">Ten Gurus and Authority</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ten Gurus and Authority">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sikh_gurus" title="Sikh gurus">Sikh gurus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1172" data-file-width="887" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg/170px-Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg/255px-Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg/340px-Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A rare <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanjore_painting" title="Tanjore painting">Tanjore</a>-style painting from the late 19th century depicting the ten Sikh Gurus with <a href="/wiki/Bhai_Bala" title="Bhai Bala">Bhai Bala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhai_Mardana" title="Bhai Mardana">Bhai Mardana</a></div></div></div>
<p>The term <a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">Guru</a> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <i>gurū</i>, meaning teacher, enlightener, guide, or mentor. The traditions and philosophy of Sikhism were established by ten Gurus from 1469 to 1708.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panjab_Publishers_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Panjab_Publishers-114">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nesbitt2005_a_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nesbitt2005_a-115">[110]</a></sup> Each Guru added to and reinforced the message taught by the previous, resulting in the creation of the Sikh religion. Guru Nanak was the first Guru and appointed a disciple as successor. <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a> was the final Guru in human form. Before his death, Guru Gobind Singh decreed in 1708, that the Gurū Granth Sāhib would be the final and perpetual Guru of the Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-granthfinalguru_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-granthfinalguru-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Guru Nanak stated that his Guru is God who is the same from the beginning of time to the end of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[111]</a></sup> Nanak said to be a God's slave and servant, but maintained that he was only a guide and teacher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ksingh116_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-ksingh116-117">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[113]</a></sup> Nanak stated that the human Guru is mortal, who is to be respected and loved but not worshipped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ksingh116_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-ksingh116-117">[112]</a></sup> When Guru, or SatGuru (The true Guru) is used in <i>Gurbani</i> it is often referring to the highest expression of truthfulness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Angad" title="Guru Angad">Guru Angad</a> succeeded Guru Nanak. Later, an important phase in the development of Sikhism came with the third successor, Guru Amar Das. Guru Nanak's teachings emphasised the pursuit of salvation; Guru Amar Das began building a cohesive community of followers with initiatives such as sanctioning distinctive ceremonies for birth, marriage, and death. Amar Das also established the <i>manji</i> (comparable to a <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">diocese</a>) system of clerical supervision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p254_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-p254-61">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Amar_Das" title="Guru Amar Das">Guru Amar Das</a>'s successor and son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Guru_Ram_Das" title="Guru Ram Das">Guru Ram Das</a> founded the city of <a href="/wiki/Amritsar" title="Amritsar">Amritsar</a>, which is home of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harimandir_Sahib" title="Harimandir Sahib">Harimandir Sahib</a> and regarded widely as the holiest city for all Sikhs. <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> was arrested by <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal</a> authorities who were suspicious and hostile to the religious community he was developing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[115]</a></sup> His persecution and death inspired his successors to promote a military and political organization of Sikh communities to defend themselves against the attacks of Mughal forces.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1188" data-file-width="1727" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg/220px-Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg/330px-Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg/440px-Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The interior of the <a href="/wiki/Akal_Takht" title="Akal Takht">Akal Takht</a></div></div></div><p>The Sikh gurus established a mechanism which allowed the Sikh religion to react as a community to changing circumstances. The sixth guru, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guru_Har_Gobind" title="Guru Har Gobind">Guru Hargobind</a>, was responsible for the creation of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Akal_Takht" title="Akal Takht">Akal Takht</a> (<i>throne of the timeless one</i>), which serves as the supreme decision-making centre of Sikhism and sits opposite the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harmandir_Sahib" title="Harmandir Sahib">Harmandir Sahib</a>. The Akal Takht is located in the city of Amritsar. The leader is appointed by the Shiromani Gurdwara Pabandhak Committee (SPGC). The <i><a href="/wiki/Sarbat_Khalsa" title="Sarbat Khalsa">Sarbat Ḵẖālsā</a></i> (a representative portion of the Khalsa Panth) historically gathers at the Akal Takht on special festivals such as <a href="/wiki/Vaisakhi" title="Vaisakhi">Vaisakhi</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hola_Mohalla" title="Hola Mohalla">Hola Mohalla</a> and when there is a need to discuss matters that affect the entire Sikh nation. A <i><a href="/wiki/Gurmata" title="Gurmata">gurmatā</a></i> (literally, 'guru's intention') is an order passed by the Sarbat Ḵẖālsā in the presence of the Gurū Granth Sāhib. A <i>gurmatā</i> may only be passed on a subject that affects the fundamental principles of Sikh religion; it is binding upon all Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[116]</a></sup> The term <i><a href="/wiki/Hukamnama" title="Hukamnama">hukamnāmā</a></i> (literally, 'edict' or 'royal order') is often used interchangeably with the term <i>gurmatā</i>. However, a <i>hukamnāmā</i> formally refers to a hymn from the <i>Gurū Granth Sāhib</i> which is given order to Sikhs.
</p><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SikhGurus-LifeSpans-GurushipSpans.jpg"><img alt="Graph showing Life Spans and Guruship Spans of Sikh Gurus" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="611" data-file-width="929" decoding="async" height="526" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/SikhGurus-LifeSpans-GurushipSpans.jpg/800px-SikhGurus-LifeSpans-GurushipSpans.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/SikhGurus-LifeSpans-GurushipSpans.jpg 1.5x" width="800"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SikhGurus-LifeSpans-GurushipSpans.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Approximate Life Spans and Guruship Spans of the 10 Sikh Gurus</div></div></div></div>
<p>The word <i>guru</i> in Sikhism also refers to <i>Akal Purkh</i> (God), and God and <i>guru</i> can sometimes be synonymous in <i><a href="/wiki/Gurbani" title="Gurbani">Gurbani</a></i> (Sikh writings).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panjab_Publishers_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Panjab_Publishers-114">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scripture">Scripture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Scripture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is one primary scripture for the Sikhs: the <i>Gurū Granth Sāhib</i>. It is sometimes synonymously referred to as the <i>Ādi Granth</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-123">[118]</a></sup> Chronologically, however, the <i>Ādi Granth</i> – literally, 'First Volume' – refers to the version of the scripture created by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> in 1604.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[119]</a></sup> The <i>Gurū Granth Sāhib</i> is the final expanded version of the scripture compiled by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-123">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[120]</a></sup> While the <i>Guru Granth Sahib</i> is an unquestioned scripture in Sikhism, another important religious text, the <i><a href="/wiki/Dasam_Granth" title="Dasam Granth">Dasam Granth</a></i>, does not enjoy universal consensus, but is considered a secondary scripture by many Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-123">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adi_Granth"><i>Adi Granth</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Adi Granth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C4%80di_Granth" title="Ādi Granth">Ādi Granth</a></div>
<p>The <i>Ādi Granth</i> was compiled primarily by <a href="/wiki/Bhai_Gurdas" title="Bhai Gurdas">Bhai Gurdas</a> under the supervision of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> between the years 1603 and 1604.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[121]</a></sup> It is written in the <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhī</a> script, which is a descendant of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La%E1%B9%87%E1%B8%8D%C4%81" title="Laṇḍā">Laṇḍā</a> script used in the Punjab at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[122]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhī</a> script was standardised by Guru Angad, the second guru of the Sikhs, for use in the Sikh scriptures and is thought to have been influenced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C4%81rad%C4%81_script" title="Śāradā script">Śāradā</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devan%C4%81gar%C4%AB" title="Devanāgarī">Devanāgarī</a> scripts. An authoritative scripture was created to protect the integrity of hymns and teachings of the Sikh Gurus, and thirteen Hindu and two Muslim bhagats of the <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_movement" title="Bhakti movement">Bhakti movement</a> sant tradition in medieval India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[123]</a></sup> The thirteen Hindu <i>bhagats</i> whose teachings were entered into the text included <a href="/wiki/Ramananda" title="Ramananda">Ramananda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Namdev" title="Namdev">Namdev</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Pipa" title="Bhagat Pipa">Pipa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ravidas" title="Ravidas">Ravidas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Beni" title="Bhagat Beni">Beni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Bhikhan" title="Bhagat Bhikhan">Bhikhan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Dhanna" title="Bhagat Dhanna">Dhanna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jayadeva" title="Jayadeva">Jaidev</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Parmanand" title="Bhagat Parmanand">Parmanand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Sadhana" title="Bhagat Sadhana">Sadhana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Sain" title="Bhagat Sain">Sain</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sur_(poet)" title="Sur (poet)">Sur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Trilochan" title="Bhagat Trilochan">Trilochan</a>, while the two Muslim <i>bhagats</i> were <a href="/wiki/Kabir" title="Kabir">Kabir</a> and Sufi saint <a href="/wiki/Fariduddin_Ganjshakar" title="Fariduddin Ganjshakar">Farid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro924_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro924-129">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[127]</a></sup> However, the bhagats in context often spoke of transcending their religious labels, Kabir often attributed to being a Muslim states in the <i>Adi Granth</i>, "I am not Hindu nor Muslim."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[128]</a></sup> The Gurus following on this message taught that different methods of devotion are for the same infinite God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Guru_Granth_Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Guru Granth Sahib">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="847" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg/170px-Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg/255px-Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg/340px-Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gurū Granth Sāhib – the primary scripture of Sikhism</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gur%C5%AB_Granth_S%C4%81hib" title="Gurū Granth Sāhib">Gurū Granth Sāhib</a></div>
<p>The Guru Granth Sahib is the holy scripture of the Sikhs, and is regarded as the living Guru.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Compilation">Compilation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Compilation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Guru Granth started as a volume of Guru Nanak's poetic compositions. Prior to his death, he passed on his volume to Guru Angad (Guru 1539–1551). The final version of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gur%C5%AB_Granth_S%C4%81hib" title="Gurū Granth Sāhib">Gurū Granth Sāhib</a> was compiled by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a> in 1678. It consists of the original Ādi Granth with the addition of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Guru Tegh Bahadur</a>'s hymns. The predominant bulk of Guru Granth Sahib is compositions by seven Sikh Gurus – Guru Nanak, Guru Angad, Guru Amar Das, Guru Ram Das, Guru Arjan, Guru Teg Bahadur and Guru Gobind Singh. It also contains the traditions and teachings of thirteen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> Bhakti movement <i>sants</i> (saints) such as <a href="/wiki/Ramananda" title="Ramananda">Ramananda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Namdev" title="Namdev">Namdev</a> among others, and two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> saints namely <a href="/wiki/Kabir" title="Kabir">Kabir</a> and the Sufi <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheikh_Farid" title="Sheikh Farid">Sheikh Farid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro924_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro924-129">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p254_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-p254-61">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The text comprises 6,000 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shabhad" title="Shabhad">śabads</a></i> (line compositions),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_123-3"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-123">[118]</a></sup> which are poetically rendered and set to rhythmic ancient north Indian classical music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-king359_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-king359-135">[130]</a></sup> The bulk of the scripture is classified into sixty <i><a href="/wiki/Raga" title="Raga">rāgas</a></i>, with each Granth rāga subdivided according to length and author. The hymns in the scripture are arranged primarily by the <i>rāgas</i> in which they are read.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_123-4"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-123">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_and_script">Language and script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Language and script">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guru_Har_Rai_-_Mool_Mantar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="286" data-file-width="376" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Guru_Har_Rai_-_Mool_Mantar.jpg/250px-Guru_Har_Rai_-_Mool_Mantar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Guru_Har_Rai_-_Mool_Mantar.jpg/375px-Guru_Har_Rai_-_Mool_Mantar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/05/Guru_Har_Rai_-_Mool_Mantar.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guru_Har_Rai_-_Mool_Mantar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mul_Mantar" title="Mul Mantar">Mul Mantar</a> written by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Har_Rai" title="Guru Har Rai">Guru Har Rai</a>, showing the Ik Onkar at top.</div></div></div>
<p>The main language used in the scripture is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Sant_Bhasha" title="Sant Bhasha">Sant Bhāṣā</a></i>, a language related to both <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindi_language" title="Hindi language">Hindi</a> and used extensively across medieval northern India by proponents of popular devotional religion (<a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">bhakti</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parrinderp259_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-parrinderp259-136">[131]</a></sup> The text is printed in Gurumukhi script, believed to have been developed by Guru Angad,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_123-5"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-123">[118]</a></sup>. The language shares the Indo-European roots found in numerous regional languages of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Teachings_2">Teachings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Teachings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sikh_musicians.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="514" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Sikh_musicians.jpg/170px-Sikh_musicians.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Sikh_musicians.jpg/255px-Sikh_musicians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Sikh_musicians.jpg/340px-Sikh_musicians.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sikh_musicians.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A group of <a href="/wiki/Sikh_music" title="Sikh music">Sikh musicians</a> called Dhadi at the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple" title="Golden Temple">Golden Temple</a> complex</div></div></div>
<p>The vision in the Guru Granth Sahib, states Torkel Brekke, is a society based on divine justice without oppression of any kind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brekke673_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-brekke673-138">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Granth begins with the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mul_Mantra" title="Mul Mantra">Mūl Mantra</a></i>, an iconic verse which received Guru Nanak directly from Akal Purakh (God).
The traditional Mul Mantar goes from Ik Oankar until Nanak Hosee Bhee Sach.
</p>
<dl><dd>One God exists, truth by name, creative power, without fear, without enmity, timeless form, unborn, self-existent, by the Guru's grace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[134]</a></sup></dd>
<dd>(<a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>: <span lang="pa">ੴ ਸਤਿ ਨਾਮੁ ਕਰਤਾ ਪੁਰਖੁ ਨਿਰਭਉ ਨਿਰਵੈਰੁ ਅਕਾਲ ਮੂਰਤਿ ਅਜੂਨੀ ਸੈਭੰ ਗੁਰ ਪ੍ਰਸਾਦਿ ॥</span>, <small>romanized: </small><span title="Punjabi-language romanization"><i lang="pa-Latn">Ika ōaṅkāra sati nāmu karatā purakhu nirabha'u niravairu akāla mūrati ajūnī saibhaṅ gura prasādi</i></span>)</dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="As_guru">As guru</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: As guru">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Tenth Guru, Guru Gobind Singh ji, named the Sikh scripture Guru Granth Sahib as his successor, terminating the line of human Gurus and making the scripture the literal embodiment of the eternal, impersonal Guru, where Gods/Gurus word serves as the spiritual guide for Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_McLeod_2014_page_17_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-WH_McLeod_2014_page_17-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_James_2011_pages_241-242_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-William_James_2011_pages_241-242-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-granthfinalguru_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-granthfinalguru-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>All Sikhs are commanded to take the Granth as Guru</dd>
<dd>(<a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>: <span lang="pa">ਸੱਬ ਸਿੱਖਣ ਕੋ ਹੁਕਮ ਹੈ ਗੁਰੂ ਮਾਨਯੋ ਗ੍ਰੰਥ ।</span>, <small>romanized: </small><span title="Punjabi-language romanization"><i lang="pa-Latn">Sabb sikkhaṇ kō hukam hai <a href="/wiki/Guru_Maneyo_Granth" title="Guru Maneyo Granth">gurū mānyō granth</a></i></span>)</dd></dl>
<p>The Guru Granth Sahib is installed in Sikh <i>Gurdwara</i> (temple); many Sikhs bow or prostrate before it on entering the temple. The Guru Granth Sahib is installed every morning and put to bed at night in many <i>Gurdwaras</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[136]</a></sup> The Granth is revered as eternal <i><a href="/wiki/Gurbani" title="Gurbani">gurbānī</a></i> and the spiritual authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>The copies of the Guru Granth Sahib are not regarded as material objects, but as living subjects which are alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Myrvold_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Myrvold-143">[138]</a></sup> According to Myrvold, the Sikh scripture is treated with respect like a living person, in a manner similar to the Gospel in early Christian worship. Old copies of the Sikh scripture are not thrown away, rather funerary services are performed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Myrvold_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Myrvold-143">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>In India the Guru Granth Sahib is even officially recognised by the Supreme Court of India as a judicial person which can receive donations and own land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Myrvold_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-Myrvold-143">[138]</a></sup> Yet, some Sikhs also warn that, without true comprehension of the text, veneration for the text can lead to <a href="/wiki/Bibliolatry" title="Bibliolatry">bibliolatry</a>, with the concrete form of the teachings becoming the object of worship instead of the teachings themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Myrvold_143-3"><a href="#cite_note-Myrvold-143">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_Hinduism_and_Islam">Relation to Hinduism and Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Relation to Hinduism and Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikhism_and_Hinduism" title="Sikhism and Hinduism">Sikhism and Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_Sikhism" title="Islam and Sikhism">Islam and Sikhism</a></div>
<p>The Sikh scriptures use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> terminology, with references to the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a>, and the names of gods and goddesses in Hindu bhakti movement traditions, such as Vishnu, Shiva, Brahma, Parvati, Lakshmi, Saraswati, Rama, Krishna, but not to worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brekke673_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-brekke673-138">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[139]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source.
