(December 2017)">self-published source</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[140]</a></sup> It also refers to the spiritual concepts in Hinduism (<i><a href="/wiki/Ishvara" title="Ishvara">Ishvara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagavan" title="Bhagavan">Bhagavan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a></i>) and the concept of God in <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> (<i>Allah</i>) to assert that these are just "alternate names for the Almighty One".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the Guru Granth Sahib acknowledges the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[142]</a></sup> it does not imply a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syncretic_religion" title="Syncretic religion">syncretic</a> bridge between Hinduism and Islam,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[143]</a></sup> but emphasises focusing on <a href="/wiki/Nitnem" title="Nitnem">nitnem banis</a> like <a href="/wiki/Japji_Sahib" title="Japji Sahib">Japu</a> (repeating <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantra</a> of the divine Name of God – <a href="/wiki/Waheguru" title="Waheguru">Waheguru</a>), instead of Muslim practices such as <a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">circumcision</a> or praying by prostrating on the ground to God, or Hindu rituals such as wearing thread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dasam_Granth">Dasam Granth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Dasam Granth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3582" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg/220px-Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg/330px-Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg/440px-Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Dasam Granth is a Sikh scripture which contains texts attributed to Guru Gobind Singh, including his autobiography <a href="/wiki/Bachittar_Natak" title="Bachittar Natak">Bachittar Natak</a>. The major narrative in the text is on <a href="/wiki/Chaubis_Avtar" title="Chaubis Avtar">Chaubis Avtar</a> (24 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avatars" title="Avatars">Avatars</a> of Hindu god <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>), <a href="/wiki/Rudra" title="Rudra">Rudra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>, the Hindu warrior goddess <a href="/wiki/Chandi" title="Chandi">Chandi</a> and a story of <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Rama</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bachittar_Natak" title="Bachittar Natak">Bachittar Natak</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jdeol31_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-jdeol31-150">[145]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dasam_Granth" title="Dasam Granth">Dasam Granth</a></div>
<p>The Dasam Granth is a scripture of Sikhs which contains texts attributed to the Guru Gobind Singh. The <i>Dasam Granth</i> is important to a great number of Sikhs, however it does not have the same authority as the <i>Guru Granth Sahib</i>. Some compositions of the <i>Dasam Granth</i> like <a href="/wiki/Jaap_Sahib" title="Jaap Sahib">Jaap Sahib</a>, (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amrit_Savaiye" title="Amrit Savaiye">Amrit Savaiye</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Chaupai_(Sikhism)" title="Chaupai (Sikhism)">Benti Chaupai</a> are part of the daily prayers (<a href="/wiki/Nitnem" title="Nitnem">Nitnem</a>) for Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[146]</a></sup> The first verse of the <a href="/wiki/Ard%C4%81s" title="Ardās">ardās</a> prayer is from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chandi_di_Var" title="Chandi di Var">Chandi di Var</a>. The <i>Dasam Granth</i> is largely versions of Hindu mythology from the <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a>, secular stories from a variety of sources called <i>Charitro Pakhyan</i> – tales to protect careless men from perils of lust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Five versions of <i>Dasam Granth</i> exist, and the authenticity of the <i>Dasam Granth</i> has in modern times become one of the most debated topics within Sikhism. The text played a significant role in Sikh history, but in modern times parts of the text have seen antipathy and discussion among Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jdeol31_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-jdeol31-150">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Janamsakhis">Janamsakhis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Janamsakhis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Janams%C4%81kh%C4%ABs" title="Janamsākhīs">Janamsākhīs</a></div>
<p>The Janamsākhīs (literally <i>birth stories</i>), are writings which profess to be biographies of Guru Nanak. Although not scripture in the strictest sense, they provide a <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiographic</a> look at Guru Nanak's life and the early start of Sikhism. There are several – often contradictory and sometimes unreliable – Janamsākhīs and they are not held in the same regard as other sources of scriptural knowledge.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observances">Observances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Observances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg/220px-InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg/330px-InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg/440px-InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Darbar_Sahib_Hall" title="Darbar Sahib Hall">Darbar Sahib</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Gurdwara" title="Gurdwara">Gurdwara</a></div></div></div>
<p>Observant Sikhs adhere to long-standing practices and traditions to strengthen and express their faith. The daily recitation of the divine name of God VaheGuru and from a memory of specific passages from the Gurū Granth Sāhib, like the <i>Japu</i> (or <i>Japjī</i>, literally <i>chant</i>) hymns is recommended immediately after rising and bathing. Baptized Sikhs recite the five-morning prayers, the evening and night prayer. Family customs include both reading passages from the scripture and attending the <a href="/wiki/Gurdwara" title="Gurdwara">gurdwara</a> (also <i>gurduārā</i>, meaning <i>the doorway to God</i>; sometimes transliterated as <i>Gurudwara</i>). There are many gurdwaras prominently constructed and maintained across India, as well as in almost every nation where Sikhs reside. Gurdwaras are open to all, regardless of religion, background, caste, or race.
</p><p>Worship in a gurdwara consists chiefly of the singing of passages from the scripture. Sikhs will commonly enter the gurdwara, touch the ground before the holy scripture with their foreheads. The recitation of the eighteenth century <i><a href="/wiki/Ard%C4%81s" title="Ardās">ardās</a></i> is also customary for attending Sikhs. The ardās recalls past sufferings and glories of the community, invoking divine grace for all humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p260_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-p260-154">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>The gurdwara is also the location for the historic Sikh practice of "<a href="/wiki/Langar_(Sikhism)" title="Langar (Sikhism)">Langar</a>" or the community meal. All gurdwaras are open to anyone of any faith for a free meal, always vegetarian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[150]</a></sup> People eat together, and the kitchen is maintained and serviced by Sikh community volunteers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Sikh_festivals.2Fevents"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikh_festivals/events">Sikh festivals/events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sikh festivals/events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Amar_Das" title="Guru Amar Das">Guru Amar Das</a> chose festivals for celebration by Sikhs like <a href="/wiki/Vaisakhi" title="Vaisakhi">Vaisakhi</a>, wherein he asked Sikhs to assemble and share the festivities as a community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColeSambhi1995p135_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColeSambhi1995p135-157">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vaisakhi is one of the most important festivals of Sikhs, while other significant festivals commemorate the birth, lives of the Gurus and Sikh martyrs. Historically, these festivals have been based on the moon calendar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bikrami_calendar" title="Bikrami calendar">Bikrami calendar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nesbittbc_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-nesbittbc-159">[154]</a></sup> In 2003, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SGPC" title="SGPC">SGPC</a>, the Sikh organisation in charge of upkeep of the historical gurdwaras of Punjab, adopted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanakshahi" title="Nanakshahi">Nanakshahi</a> calendar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nesbittbc_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-nesbittbc-159">[154]</a></sup> The new calendar is highly controversial among Sikhs and is not universally accepted. Sikh festivals include the following:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vaisakhi" title="Vaisakhi">Vaisakhi</a> which includes Parades and Nagar Kirtan and occurs on 13 April or 14 April. Sikhs celebrate it because on this day, which fell on 30 March 1699, the tenth Guru, Gobind Singh, inaugurated the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a>, the 11th body of Guru Granth Sahib and leader of Sikhs until eternity.
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nagar_Kirtan" title="Nagar Kirtan">Nagar Kirtan</a> involves the processional singing of holy hymns throughout a community. While practiced at any time, it is customary in the month of Visakhi (or Vaisakhi). Traditionally, the procession is led by the saffron-robed Panj Piare (the five beloved of the Guru), who are followed by the Guru Granth Sahib, the holy Sikh scripture, which is placed on a float.</li></ul></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:768px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg" title="Nagar Kirtan crowd listening to Kirtan at Yuba City, California."><img alt="" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="1276" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg/760px-Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg/1140px-Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg 2x" width="760"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg" title="File:Yuba City Nagar Kirtan 2011.jpg"> </a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nagar_Kirtan" title="Nagar Kirtan">Nagar Kirtan</a> crowd listening to <a href="/wiki/Kirtan" title="Kirtan">Kirtan</a> at <a href="/wiki/Yuba_City,_California" title="Yuba City, California">Yuba City, California</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Band Chor Diwas has been another important Sikh festival in its history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[155]</a></sup> In recent years, instead of Diwali, the post-2003 calendar released by SGPC has named it the <a href="/wiki/Bandi_Chhor_Divas" title="Bandi Chhor Divas">Bandi Chhor divas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[156]</a></sup> Sikhs celebrate <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a>'s release from the <a href="/wiki/Gwalior_Fort" title="Gwalior Fort">Gwalior Fort</a>, with several innocent Raja kings who were also imprisoned by Mughal Emperor <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a> in 1619. This day continues to be commemorated on the same day of Hindu festival of <a href="/wiki/Diwali" title="Diwali">Diwali</a>, with lights, fireworks and festivities.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hola_Mohalla" title="Hola Mohalla">Hola Mohalla</a> is a tradition started by Guru Gobind Singh. It starts the day after Sikhs celebrate <a href="/wiki/Holi" title="Holi">Holi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2009p95_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2009p95-162">[157]</a></sup> sometimes referred to as <i>Hola</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roy2005p192_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roy2005p192-163">[158]</a></sup> Guru Gobind Singh modified Holi with a three-day <a href="/wiki/Hola_Mohalla" title="Hola Mohalla">Hola Mohalla</a> extension festival of martial arts. The extension started the day after the Holi festival in <a href="/wiki/Anandpur_Sahib" title="Anandpur Sahib">Anandpur Sahib</a>, where Sikh soldiers would train in mock battles, compete in horsemanship, athletics, archery and military exercises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[160]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gurpurb" title="Gurpurb">Gurpurbs</a> are celebrations or commemorations based on the lives of the Sikh Gurus. They tend to be either birthdays or celebrations of Sikh martyrdom. All ten Gurus have Gurpurbs on the Nanakshahi calendar, but it is Guru Nanak and Guru Gobind Singh who have a gurpurb that is widely celebrated in Gurdwaras and Sikh homes. The martyrdoms are also known as a Shaheedi Gurpurbs, which mark the martyrdom anniversary of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Guru Tegh Bahadur</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ceremonies_and_customs">Ceremonies and customs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Ceremonies and customs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sikh_wedding.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sikh_wedding.jpg/220px-Sikh_wedding.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sikh_wedding.jpg/330px-Sikh_wedding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sikh_wedding.jpg/440px-Sikh_wedding.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sikh_wedding.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikh_wedding" title="Sikh wedding">Sikh wedding</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi,_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg/220px-Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg/330px-Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg/440px-Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi,_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sikh funeral procession, <a href="/wiki/Mandi,_Himachal_Pradesh" title="Mandi, Himachal Pradesh">Mandi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Himachal_Pradesh" title="Himachal Pradesh">Himachal Pradesh</a></div></div></div>
<p>Khalsa Sikhs have also supported and helped develop major pilgrimage traditions to sacred sites such as Harmandir Sahib, Anandpur Sahib, Fatehgarh Sahib, Patna Sahib, Hazur Nanded Sahib, Hemkund Sahib and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994p68_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994p68-166">[161]</a></sup> Sikh pilgrims and Sikhs of other sects customarily consider these as holy and a part of their <i>Tirath</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferrari2011p48_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferrari2011p48-167">[162]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hola_Mohalla" title="Hola Mohalla">Hola Mohalla</a> around the festival of <a href="/wiki/Holi" title="Holi">Holi</a>, for example, is a ceremonial and customary gathering every year in <a href="/wiki/Anandpur_Sahib" title="Anandpur Sahib">Anandpur Sahib</a> attracting over 100,000 Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[164]</a></sup> Major Sikh temples feature a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarovar" title="Sarovar">sarovar</a></i> where some Sikhs take a customary dip. Some take home the <a href="/wiki/Sacred_waters" title="Sacred waters">sacred water</a> of the tank particularly for sick friends and relatives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singha7_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-singha7-170">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[166]</a></sup> believing that the waters of such sacred sites have restorative powers and the ability to purify one's <i>karma</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[vi]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singha7_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-singha7-170">[165]</a></sup> The various Gurus of Sikhism have had different approaches to pilgrimage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>Upon a child's birth, the Guru Granth Sahib is opened at a random point and the child is named using the first letter on the top left hand corner of the left page. All boys are given the last name <a href="/wiki/Singh" title="Singh">Singh</a>, and all girls are given the last name <a href="/wiki/Kaur" title="Kaur">Kaur</a> (this was once a title which was conferred on an individual upon joining the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sikh marriage ritual includes the <i><a href="/wiki/Anand_Karaj" title="Anand Karaj">anand kāraj</a></i> ceremony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruether2006p700_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruether2006p700-177">[171]</a></sup> The marriage ceremony is performed in front of the Guru Granth Sahib by a baptized Khalsa, Granthi of the Gurdwara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaarKalsi2009p10_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-HaarKalsi2009p10-178">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenech33_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-fenech33-179">[173]</a></sup> The tradition of circling the Guru Granth Sahib and Anand Karaj among Khalsa is practised since the fourth Guru, Guru Ram Das. Its official recognition and adoption came in 1909, during the <a href="/wiki/Singh_Sabha_Movement" title="Singh Sabha Movement">Singh Sabha Movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenech33_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-fenech33-179">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>Upon death, the body of a Sikh is usually cremated. If this is not possible, any respectful means of disposing the body may be employed. The <i>kīrtan sōhilā</i> and <i>ardās</i> prayers are performed during the funeral ceremony (known as <i><a href="/wiki/Antam_Sanskar" title="Antam Sanskar">antim sanskār</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initiation_and_the_Khalsa">Initiation and the Khalsa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Initiation and the Khalsa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a> (meaning "pure and sovereign") is the collective name given by Guru Gobind Singh to those Sikhs who have been fully initiated by taking part in a ceremony called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammrit_sa%C3%B1c%C4%81r" title="Ammrit sañcār">ammrit sañcār</a></i> (nectar ceremony).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-181">[175]</a></sup> During this ceremony, sweetened water is stirred with a double-edged sword while liturgical prayers are sung; it is offered to the initiating Sikh, who ritually drinks it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-181">[175]</a></sup> Many Sikhs are not formally and fully initiated, as they do not undergo this ceremony, but do adhere to some components of Sikhism and identify as Sikhs. The initiated Sikh, who is believed to be <a href="/wiki/Dvija#The_meaning_of_the_two_births" title="Dvija">reborn</a>, is referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amritdhari" title="Amritdhari">Amritdhari</a> or Khalsa Sikh, while those who are not initiated or baptised are referred to as Kesdhari or Sahajdhari Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_181-2"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-181">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first time that this ceremony took place was on <a href="/wiki/Vaisakhi" title="Vaisakhi">Vaisakhi</a>, which fell on 30 March 1699 at <a href="/wiki/Anandpur_Sahib" title="Anandpur Sahib">Anandpur Sahib</a> in Punjab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_181-3"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-181">[175]</a></sup> It was on that occasion that Gobind Singh baptised the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panj_Piare" title="Panj Piare">Pañj Piārē</a> – the five beloved ones, who in turn baptised Guru Gobind Singh himself. To males who initiated, the last name Singh, meaning "lion", was given, while the last name Kaur, meaning "princess", was given to baptised Sikh females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_181-4"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-181">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>Baptised <a href="/wiki/Sikhs" title="Sikhs">Sikhs</a> wear five items, called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Ks" title="Five Ks">Five Ks</a> (in Punjabi known as <i>pañj kakkē</i> or <i>pañj kakār</i>), at all times. The five items are: <i><a href="/wiki/Kesh_(Sikhism)" title="Kesh (Sikhism)">kēs</a></i> (uncut hair), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanga_(Sikhism)" title="Kanga (Sikhism)">kaṅghā</a></i> (small wooden comb), <i><a href="/wiki/Kara_(Sikhism)" title="Kara (Sikhism)">kaṛā</a></i> (circular steel or iron bracelet), <i><a href="/wiki/Kirpan" title="Kirpan">kirpān</a></i> (sword/dagger), and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaccha" title="Kaccha">kacchera</a></i> (special undergarment).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_181-5"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-181">[175]</a></sup> The Five Ks have both practical and symbolic purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sikhism" title="History of Sikhism">History of Sikhism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Punjab_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="585" data-file-width="955" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Punjab_map.svg/300px-Punjab_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Punjab_map.svg/450px-Punjab_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Punjab_map.svg/600px-Punjab_map.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Punjab_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Punjab, where Sikhism originated, against present-day borders</div></div></div>
<p>Guru Nanak (1469–1539), the founder of Sikhism, was born in the village of <i>Rāi Bhōi dī Talwandī</i>, now called <a href="/wiki/Nankana_Sahib" title="Nankana Sahib">Nankana Sahib</a> (in present-day Pakistan).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[178]</a></sup> His parents were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjabi_Khatri" title="Punjabi Khatri">Punjabi Khatri</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[180]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiography</a> <i>Puratan Janamsakhi</i> composed more than two centuries after his death and probably based on <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[181]</a></sup> Nanak as a boy was fascinated by religion and spiritual matters, spending time with wandering ascetics and holy men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shacklexiii_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexiii-188">[182]</a></sup> His friend was Mardana, a Muslim. Together they would sing devotional songs all night in front of the public, and bathe in the river in the morning. One day, at the usual bath, Nanak went missing and his family feared he had drowned. Three days later he returned home, and declared: "There is no Hindu, there is no Muslim" ("<i>nā kōi hindū nā kōi musalmān</i>"). Thereafter, Nanak started preaching his ideas that form the tenets of Sikhism. In 1526, Guru Nanak at age 50, started a small commune in Kartarpur and his disciples came to be known as <i>Sikhs</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shacklexiii_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexiii-188">[182]</a></sup> Although the exact account of his itinerary is disputed, hagiographic accounts state he made five major journeys, spanning thousands of miles: the first tour being east towards <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>; the second south towards <a href="/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh" title="Andhra Pradesh">Andhra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>; the third north to <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Meru" title="Mount Meru">Mount Sumeru</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[183]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>; and the fourth to <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[184]</a></sup> In his last and final tour, he returned to the banks of the Ravi River to end his days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two competing theories on Guru Nanak's teachings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arvindmandair131_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-arvindmandair131-192">[186]</a></sup> One, according to Cole and Sambhi, is based on hagiographical <a href="/wiki/Janamsakhis" title="Janamsakhis">Janamsakhis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[187]</a></sup> and states that Nanak's teachings and Sikhism were a revelation from God, and not a social protest movement nor any attempt to reconcile Hinduism and Islam in the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_Sambhi_Dic_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cole_Sambhi_Dic-194">[188]</a></sup> The other states that Nanak was a <a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a>. According to Singha, "Sikhism does not subscribe to the theory of incarnation or the concept of prophethood. But it has a pivotal concept of Guru. He is not an incarnation of God, not even a prophet. He is an illumined soul."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HS_Singha_2009_page_104_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-HS_Singha_2009_page_104-195">[189]</a></sup> The second theory continues that hagiographical <i>Janamsakhis</i> were not written by Nanak, but by later followers without regard for historical accuracy, and contain numerous legends and myths created to show respect for Nanak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[190]</a></sup> The term <i>revelation</i>, clarify Cole and Sambhi, in Sikhism is not limited to the teachings of Nanak, but is extended to all Sikh gurus, as well as the words of past, present and future men and women, who possess divine knowledge intuitively through meditation. The Sikh revelations include the words of non-Sikh <a href="/wiki/Bhagat" title="Bhagat">bhagats</a>, some who lived and died before the birth of Nanak, and whose teachings are part of the Sikh scriptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[191]</a></sup> The Adi Granth and successive Sikh gurus repeatedly emphasised, states Mandair, that Sikhism is "not about hearing voices from God, but it is about changing the nature of the human mind, and anyone can achieve direct experience and spiritual perfection at any time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arvindmandair131_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-arvindmandair131-192">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_influences">Historical influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Historical influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The roots of the Sikh tradition are, states Louis Fenech, perhaps in the <a href="/wiki/Sant_(religion)" title="Sant (religion)">Sant</a>-tradition of India whose ideology grew to become the Bhakti tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35-198">[vii]</a></sup> Furthermore, adds Fenech:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Few Sikhs would mention these Indic texts and ideologies in the same breadth as the Sikh tradition, let alone trace elements of their tradition to this chronological and ideological point, <em>despite the fact</em> that the Indic mythology permeates the Sikh sacred canon, the <i>Guru Granth Sahib</i>, and the secondary canon, the <i>Dasam Granth</i> ... and adds delicate nuance and substance to the sacred symbolic universe of the Sikhs of today and of their past ancestors.</p></blockquote>
<p>The development of Sikhism was influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_movement" title="Bhakti movement">Bhakti movement</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Lorenzen_1995_pages_1-2_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-David_Lorenzen_1995_pages_1-2-200">[viii]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35-198">[vii]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclobritannicasikh_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyclobritannicasikh-201">[ix]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitagawa2013_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kitagawa2013-202">[193]</a></sup> however, Sikhism was not simply an extension of the Bhakti movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singha_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Singha-88">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pruthi_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pruthi-203">[194]</a></sup> Sikhism, for instance, disagreed with some of the views of Bhakti saints Kabir and Ravidas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[x]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pruthi_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pruthi-203">[194]</a></sup> Sikhism developed while the region was being ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>. Two of the Sikh Gurus, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Guru Tegh Bahadur</a>, refused to convert to Islam and were tortured and executed by the Mughal rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[195]</a></sup> The Islamic era persecution of Sikhs triggered the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a>, as an order for freedom of conscience and religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gandhi_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gandhi-36">[33]</a></sup> A Sikh is expected to embody the qualities of a "Sant-Sipāhī" –  a <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">saint-soldier</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chanchreek_2007_142_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chanchreek_2007_142-37">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dugga_2001_33_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dugga_2001_33-38">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_of_Sikhism">Growth of Sikhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Growth of Sikhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="777" data-file-width="777" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG/220px-Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG/330px-Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG/440px-Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> explaining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikh_beliefs" title="Sikh beliefs">Sikh teachings</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sadhu" title="Sadhu">Sadhus</a></div></div></div>
<p>After its inception, Sikhism grew as it gained converts among Hindus and Muslims in the Punjab region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2008_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2008-30">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh1989_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh1989-207">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh1996_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh1996-209">[199]</a></sup> In 1539, Guru Nanak chose his disciple Lahiṇā as a successor to the Guruship rather than either of his sons. Lahiṇā was named <a href="/wiki/Guru_Angad" title="Guru Angad">Guru Angad</a> and became the second Guru of the Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenech2014p36_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fenech2014p36-211">[201]</a></sup> Nanak conferred his choice at the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kartarpur_(Pakistan)" title="Kartarpur (Pakistan)">Kartarpur</a> on the banks of the river <a href="/wiki/Ravi_River" title="Ravi River">Ravi</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sri_Chand" title="Sri Chand">Sri Chand</a>, Guru Nanak's son was also a religious man, and continued his own commune of Sikhs. His followers came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Udasi" title="Udasi">Udasi</a> Sikhs, the first parallel sect of Sikhism that formed in Sikh history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994p78_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994p78-212">[202]</a></sup> The Udasis believe that the Guruship should have gone to Sri Chand, since he was a man of pious habits in addition to being Nanak's son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994p78_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994p78-212">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>Guru Angad, before joining Guru Nanak's commune, worked as a <i><a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">pujari</a></i> (priest) and religious teacher centered around Hindu goddess <a href="/wiki/Durga" title="Durga">Durga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenech2014p36_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fenech2014p36-211">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColeSambhi1995p18_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColeSambhi1995p18-213">[203]</a></sup> On Nanak's advice, Guru Angad moved from Kartarpur to Khadur, where his wife <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mata_Khivi" title="Mata Khivi">Khivi</a> and children were living, until he was able to bridge the divide between his followers and the Udasis. Guru Angad continued the work started by Guru Nanak and is widely credited for standardising the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gurmukh%C4%AB_script" title="Gurmukhī script">Gurmukhī script</a> as used in the sacred scripture of the Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColeSambhi1995p18_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-ColeSambhi1995p18-213">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Amar_Das" title="Guru Amar Das">Guru Amar Das</a> became the third Sikh Guru in 1552 at the age of 73. He adhered to the <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a> tradition of Hinduism for much of his life, before joining the commune of Guru Angad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FenechMcLeod2014p29_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FenechMcLeod2014p29-214">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[205]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Goindval" title="Goindval">Goindval</a> became an important centre for Sikhism during the Guruship of Guru Amar Das. He was a reformer, and discouraged veiling of women's faces (a Muslim custom) as well as <a href="/wiki/Sati_(practice)" title="Sati (practice)">sati</a> (a Hindu custom).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eosamardas_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-eosamardas-216">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[207]</a></sup> He encouraged the <a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">Kshatriya</a> people to fight in order to protect people and for the sake of justice, stating this is <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sambhi2005p29_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sambhi2005p29-218">[208]</a></sup> Guru Amar Das started the tradition of appointing <i>manji</i> (zones of religious administration with an appointed chief called <i>sangatias</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FenechMcLeod2014p29_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-FenechMcLeod2014p29-214">[204]</a></sup> introduced the <i>dasvandh</i> ("the tenth" of income) system of revenue collection in the name of Guru and as pooled community religious resource,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farhadian2015p342_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farhadian2015p342-219">[209]</a></sup> and the famed <i><a href="/wiki/Langar_(Sikhism)" title="Langar (Sikhism)">langar</a></i> tradition of Sikhism where anyone, without discrimination of any kind, could get a free meal in a communal seating. The collection of revenue from Sikhs through regional appointees helped Sikhism grow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FenechMcLeod2014p29_214-2"><a href="#cite_note-FenechMcLeod2014p29-214">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaarKalsi2009p21_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-HaarKalsi2009p21-220">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>Guru Amar Das named his disciple and son-in-law Jēṭhā as the next Guru, who came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Guru_Ram_Das" title="Guru Ram Das">Guru Ram Das</a>. The new Guru faced hostilities from the sons of Guru Amar Das and therefore shifted his official base to lands identified by Guru Amar Das as Guru-ka-Chak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandair2013p38_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair2013p38-221">[211]</a></sup> He moved his commune of Sikhs there and the place then was called Ramdaspur, after him. This city grew and later became <a href="/wiki/Amritsar" title="Amritsar">Amritsar</a> – the holiest city of Sikhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod1990p28_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod1990p28-222">[212]</a></sup> Guru Ram Das expanded the <i>manji</i> organization for clerical appointments in Sikh temples, and for revenue collections to theologically and economically support the Sikh movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandair2013p38_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair2013p38-221">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1581, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> – youngest son of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Ram_Das" title="Guru Ram Das">Guru Ram Das</a>, became the fifth Guru of the Sikhs. The choice of successor, as throughout most of the history of Sikh Guru successions, led to disputes and internal divisions among the Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShackleMandair2013xv_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShackleMandair2013xv-223">[213]</a></sup> The elder son of Guru Ram Das named <a href="/wiki/Prithi_Chand" title="Prithi Chand">Prithi Chand</a> is remembered in the Sikh tradition as vehemently opposing Guru Arjan, creating a faction Sikh community which the Sikhs following Guru Arjan called as <i>Minas</i> (literally, "scoundrels").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenech39_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-fenech39-224">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2009p20_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2009p20-225">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>Guru Arjan is remembered in the Sikh for many things. He built the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harimandir_Sahib" title="Harimandir Sahib">Harimandir Sahib</a> (later to become the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple" title="Golden Temple">Golden Temple</a>). He was a poet and created the first edition of Sikh sacred text known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adi_Granth" title="Adi Granth">Ādi Granth</a> (literally "the first book") and included the writings of the first five Gurus and other enlightened 13 Hindu and 2 Muslim Sufi saints. In 1606, he was tortured and killed by the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal</a> emperor <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[216]</a></sup> for refusing to convert to Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[218]</a></sup> His martyrdom is considered a watershed event in the history of Sikhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_advancement">Political advancement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Political advancement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the martyrdom of Guru Arjan, his son <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a> at age eleven became the sixth Guru of the Sikhs, and Sikhism dramatically evolved to become a political movement in addition to being religious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pashaura29_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-pashaura29-230">[220]</a></sup> Guru Hargobind carried two swords, calling one spiritual and the other for temporal purpose (known as <i>mīrī</i> and <i>pīrī</i> in Sikhism).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[221]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (December 2017)">self-published source</span></a></i>]</sup> According to the Sikh tradition, Guru Arjan asked his son Hargobind to start a military tradition to protect the Sikh people and always keep himself surrounded by armed Sikhs. The building of an armed Sikh militia began with Guru Hargobind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pashaura29_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-pashaura29-230">[220]</a></sup> Guru Hargobind was soon arrested by the Mughals and kept in jail in Gwalior. It is unclear how many years he served in prison, with different texts stating it to be between 2 and 12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mandair48_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-mandair48-232">[222]</a></sup> He married three women, built a fort to defend Ramdaspur and created a formal court called <a href="/wiki/Akal_Takht" title="Akal Takht">Akal Takht</a>, now the highest Khalsa Sikh religious authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phyllis2004_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phyllis2004-233">[223]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1644, Guru Hargobind named his grandson <a href="/wiki/Guru_Har_Rai" title="Guru Har Rai">Har Rai</a> as the Guru. The Mughal Emperor <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a> attempted political means to undermine the Sikh tradition, by dividing and influencing the succession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mandair49_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-mandair49-234">[224]</a></sup> The Mughal ruler gave land grants to Dhir Mal, a grandson of Guru Hargobind living in Kartarpur, and attempted to encourage Sikhs to recognise Dhir Mal as the rightful successor to Guru Hargobind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mandair49_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-mandair49-234">[224]</a></sup> Dhir Mal issued statements in favour of the Mughal state, and critical of his grandfather <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a>. Guru Hargobind rejected Dhir Mal, the latter refused to give up the original version of the Adi Granth he had, and the Sikh community was divided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mandair49_234-2"><a href="#cite_note-mandair49-234">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>Guru Har Rai is famed to have met Dara Shikoh during a time Dara Shikoh and his younger brother Aurangzeb were in a bitter succession fight. Aurangzeb summoned Guru Har Rai, who refused to go and sent his elder son Ram Rai instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2014p260_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2014p260-235">[225]</a></sup> The emperor found a verse in the Sikh scripture insulting to Muslims, and Ram Rai agreed it was a mistake then changed it. Ram Rai thus pleased Aurangzeb, but displeased Guru Har Rai who excommunicated his elder son. He nominated his younger son <a href="/wiki/Guru_Har_Krishan" title="Guru Har Krishan">Guru Har Krishan</a> to succeed him in 1661. Aurangzeb responded by granting Ram Rai a <a href="/wiki/Jagir" title="Jagir">jagir</a> (land grant). Ram Rai founded a town there and enjoyed Aurangzeb's patronage; the town came to be known as Dehradun, after <i>Dehra</i> referring to Ram Rai's shrine. Sikhs who followed Ram Rai came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Ramraiya" title="Ramraiya">Ramraiya</a> Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2014p260_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2014p260-235">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[226]</a></sup> However, according to rough estimates, there are around 120–150 million (12–15 <a href="/wiki/Crore" title="Crore">crore</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[227]</a></sup> Guru Har Krishan became the eighth Guru at the age of five, and died of smallpox before reaching the age of eight. No hymns composed by these three Gurus are included in the Guru Granth Sahib.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Guru Tegh Bahadur</a>, the uncle of Guru Har Krishan, became Guru in 1665. Tegh Bahadur resisted the forced conversions of <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmiri</a> <a href="/wiki/Pandit" title="Pandit">Pandits</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[229]</a></sup> and non-Muslims<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandair2013p53_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair2013p53-240">[230]</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, and was publicly beheaded in 1675 on the orders of <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a> in <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a> for refusing to convert to Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cs2013_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-cs2013-241">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenech4_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-fenech4-242">[232]</a></sup> His beheading traumatized the Sikhs. His body was cremated in Delhi, the head was carried secretively by Sikhs and cremated in <a href="/wiki/Anandpur_Sahib" title="Anandpur Sahib">Anandpur</a>. He was succeeded by his son, Gobind Rai, who militarised his followers by creating the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a> in 1699, and baptising the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panj_Piare" title="Panj Piare">Pañj Piārē</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MandairShackle2013p25_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-MandairShackle2013p25-243">[233]</a></sup> From then on, he was known as <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a>, and Sikh identity was redefined into a political force resisting religious persecution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ws1981_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-ws1981-244">[234]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
