<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikh_confederacy_and_the_rise_of_the_Khalsa">Sikh confederacy and the rise of the Khalsa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Sikh confederacy and the rise of the Khalsa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:40px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mehdiana_5.jpg" title="Sculpture at Mehdiana Sahib of the execution of Banda Singh Bahadur in 1716 by the Mughals."><img alt="Sculpture at Mehdiana Sahib of the execution of Banda Singh Bahadur in 1716 by the Mughals." data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Mehdiana_5.jpg/200px-Mehdiana_5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Mehdiana_5.jpg/300px-Mehdiana_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Mehdiana_5.jpg/400px-Mehdiana_5.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Sculpture at <a href="/wiki/Mehdiana_Sahib" title="Mehdiana Sahib">Mehdiana Sahib</a> of the execution of <a href="/wiki/Banda_Singh_Bahadur" title="Banda Singh Bahadur">Banda Singh Bahadur</a> in 1716 by the Mughals.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg" title="Some bodyguards of Maharaja Ranjit Singh at the Sikh capital, Lahore, Punjab."><img alt="Some bodyguards of Maharaja Ranjit Singh at the Sikh capital, Lahore, Punjab." data-file-height="768" data-file-width="503" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg/131px-Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg/196px-Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg/262px-Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg 2x" width="131"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Some bodyguards of <a href="/wiki/Ranjit_Singh" title="Ranjit Singh">Maharaja Ranjit Singh</a> at the Sikh capital, Lahore, Punjab.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a> inaugurated the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a> (the collective body of all <a href="/wiki/Amrit_Sanskar" title="Amrit Sanskar">initiated Sikhs</a>) as the Sikh temporal authority in the year 1699. It created a community that combines its spiritual purpose and goals with political and military duties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-granthfinalguru_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-granthfinalguru-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parrinderp259_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-parrinderp259-136">[131]</a></sup> Shortly before his death, Guru Gobind Singh proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Gurū Granth Sāhib</a> (the Sikh Holy Scripture) to be the ultimate spiritual authority for the Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sikh Khalsa's rise to power began in the 17th century during a time of growing militancy against Mughal rule. The creation of a <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Empire" title="Sikh Empire">Sikh Empire</a> began when Guru Gobind Singh sent a Sikh general, <a href="/wiki/Banda_Singh_Bahadur" title="Banda Singh Bahadur">Banda Singh Bahadur</a>, to fight the Mughal rulers of India<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[237]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (December 2017)">self-published source</span></a></i>]</sup> and those who had committed atrocities against <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pir_Buddhu_Shah&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pir Buddhu Shah (page does not exist)">Pir Buddhu Shah</a>. Banda Singh advanced his army towards the main Muslim Mughal city of Sirhind and, following the instructions of the Guru, punished all the culprits. Soon after the invasion of Sirhind, while resting in his chamber after the Rehras prayer Guru Gobind Singh was stabbed by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathan" title="Pathan">Pathan</a> assassin hired by <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughals</a>. Gobind Singh killed the attacker with his sword. Though a European surgeon stitched the Guru's wound, the wound re-opened as the Guru tugged at a hard strong bow after a few days, causing profuse bleeding that led to Gobind Singh's death.
</p><p>After the Guru's death, Baba <a href="/wiki/Banda_Singh_Bahadur" title="Banda Singh Bahadur">Banda Singh Bahadur</a> became the commander-in-chief of the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[238]</a></sup> He organised the civilian rebellion and abolished or halted the <a href="/wiki/Zamindar" title="Zamindar">Zamindari</a> system in time he was active and gave the farmers <a href="/wiki/Self-ownership" title="Self-ownership">proprietorship</a> of their own land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[239]</a></sup> Banda Singh was executed by the emperor <a href="/wiki/Farrukhsiyar" title="Farrukhsiyar">Farrukh Siyar</a> after refusing the offer of a pardon if he converted to Islam. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikh_Confederacy" title="Sikh Confederacy">confederacy of Sikh warrior bands</a> known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Misls" title="Misls">misls</a></i> emerged, but these fought between themselves. Ranjit Singh achieved a series of military victories and created a <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Empire" title="Sikh Empire">Sikh Empire</a> in 1799.
</p><p>The Sikh empire, with its capital in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, spread over almost 200,000 square miles (520,000 square kilometres) comprising what is now northwestern <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. The Sikh Empire entered into a treaty with the colonial British powers, with each side recognizing Sutlej River as the line of control and agreeing not to invade the other side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[240]</a></sup> Ranjit Singh's most lasting legacy was the restoration and expansion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harmandir_Sahib" title="Harmandir Sahib">Harmandir Sahib</a>, most revered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gurudwara" title="Gurudwara">Gurudwara</a> of the Sikhs, with marble and gold, from which the popular name of the "<a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple" title="Golden Temple">Golden Temple</a>" is derived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[241]</a></sup> After the death of Ranjit Singh in 1839, the Sikh Empire fell into disorder. Ranjit Singh had failed to establish a lasting structure for Sikh government or stable succession, and the Sikh Empire rapidly declined after his death. Factions divided the Sikhs, and led to <a href="/wiki/First_Anglo-Sikh_War" title="First Anglo-Sikh War">Anglo-Sikh wars</a>. The British easily defeated the confused and demoralised <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Khalsa_Army" title="Sikh Khalsa Army">Khalsa forces</a>, then disbanded them into destitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994p207_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994p207-252">[242]</a></sup> The youngest son of Ranjit Singh, named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maharaja_Duleep_Singh" title="Maharaja Duleep Singh">Duleep Singh</a>, ultimately succeeded, but he was arrested and exiled after the defeat of Sikh Khalsa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[243]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Singh_Sabha_movement">Singh Sabha movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Singh Sabha movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Singh_Sabha_Movement" title="Singh Sabha Movement">Singh Sabha Movement</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Singh_Sabha_movement" title="Singh Sabha movement">Singh Sabha movement</a>, a movement to revitalize Sikhism, also saw the resurgence of the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a> after their defeat in wars with the British<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[244]</a></sup> - latterly in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Sikh_War" title="Second Anglo-Sikh War">Second Anglo-Sikh War</a> - and the subsequent decline and corruption of Sikh institutions during colonial rule, and the proselytization of other faith groups in the Punjab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrierssm_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-barrierssm-255">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online-256">[246]</a></sup>  It was started in the 1870s, and after a period of interfactional rivalry, united under the Tat Khalsa to reinvigorate Sikh practice and institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perplexed84_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-perplexed84-257">[247]</a></sup>
</p><p>The last Maharaja of the Sikh Empire, Duleep Singh, converted to Christianity in 1853, a controversial but influential event in Sikh history. Along with his conversion, and after Sikh Empire had been dissolved and the region made a part of the colonial British Empire, <a href="/wiki/Proselytism" title="Proselytism">proselytising</a> activities of <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brahmo_Samaj" title="Brahmo Samaj">Brahmo Samajis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arya_Samaj" title="Arya Samaj">Arya Samaj</a>, Muslim Anjuman-i-Islamia and Ahmadiyah sought to convert the Sikhs in northwestern Indian subcontinent into their respective faiths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrierssm_255-1"><a href="#cite_note-barrierssm-255">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online-256">[246]</a></sup> These developments launched the <a href="/wiki/Singh_Sabha_Movement" title="Singh Sabha Movement">Singh Sabha Movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrierssm_255-2"><a href="#cite_note-barrierssm-255">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online_256-2"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online-256">[246]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first meeting of the movement was in the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple" title="Golden Temple">Golden Temple</a>, Amritsar in 1873, and it was largely launched by the <a href="/wiki/Sanatan_Sikh" title="Sanatan Sikh">Sanatan Sikhs</a>, Gianis, priests, and granthis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[248]</a></sup> Shortly thereafter, Nihang Sikhs began influencing the movement, followed by a sustained campaign by the <a href="/wiki/Tat_Khalsa" title="Tat Khalsa">Tat Khalsa</a>, which had quickly gained dominance by the early 1880s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perplexed84_257-1"><a href="#cite_note-perplexed84-257">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994-259">[249]</a></sup> The movement became a struggle between Sanatan Sikhs and Tat Khalsa in defining and interpreting Sikhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p28_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p28-260">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandair_2013_85–86_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair_2013_85–86-261">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenech273_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-fenech273-262">[252]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sanatan Sikhs led by <a href="/wiki/Khem_Singh_Bedi" title="Khem Singh Bedi">Khem Singh Bedi</a> – who claimed to be a direct descendant of Guru Nanak, Avtar Singh Vahiria and others supported a more inclusive approach which considered Sikhism as a reformed tradition of Hinduism, while Tat Khalsa campaigned for an exclusive approach to the Sikh identity, disagreeing with Sanatan Sikhs and seeking to modernize Sikhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenech273_262-1"><a href="#cite_note-fenech273-262">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994_259-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994-259">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandair2013p82_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair2013p82-263">[253]</a></sup> The Sikh Sabha movement expanded in north and northwest Indian subcontinent, leading to more than 100 Singh Sabhas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenech273_262-2"><a href="#cite_note-fenech273-262">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p28_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p28-260">[250]</a></sup> By the early decades of the 20th century, the influence of Tat Khalsa increased in interpreting the nature of Sikhism and their control over the Sikh Gurdwaras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenech273_262-3"><a href="#cite_note-fenech273-262">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p28_260-2"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p28-260">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994_259-2"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994-259">[249]</a></sup> The Tat Khalsa banished Brahmanical practices including the use of the <i><a href="/wiki/Yajna" title="Yajna">yagna</a></i> fire,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[255]</a></sup> replaced by the <i><a href="/wiki/Anand_Karaj" title="Anand Karaj">Anand Karaj</a></i> marriage ceremony in accordance with Sikh scripture, and the idols and the images of Sikh Gurus from the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple" title="Golden Temple">Golden Temple</a> in 1905, traditions which had taken root during the administration of the <i><a href="/wiki/Singh_Sabha_Movement#Colonial_rule" title="Singh Sabha Movement">mahants</a></i> during the 1800s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[256]</a></sup> They undertook a sustained campaign to standardize how Sikh Gurdwaras looked and ran, while looking to Sikh scriptures and the early Sikh tradition<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perplexed85_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-perplexed85-267">[257]</a></sup> to purify the Sikh identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[258]</a></sup>
</p><p>The spiritual successors of the Singh Sabha include the <a href="/wiki/Akali_movement" title="Akali movement">Akali movement</a> of the 1920s, as well as the modern-day Shiromani Gurdwara Parbandhak Committee (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SGPC" title="SGPC">SGPC</a>), a <a href="/wiki/Gurdwara" title="Gurdwara">gurdwara</a> administration body, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akali_Dal" title="Akali Dal">Akali Dal</a> political party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p30_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p30-269">[259]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Partition_of_India">Partition of India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Partition of India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sikhs participated and contributed to the decades-long Indian independence movement in the first half of the 20th century. Ultimately when the British Empire recognized independent India, the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_India" title="Partition of India">land was partitioned</a> into Hindu-majority India and Muslim-majority Pakistan (East and West) in 1947. According to Banga, the partition was a watershed event in Sikh history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100-270">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shani2007p86_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shani2007p86-271">[261]</a></sup> The Sikhs had historically lived in northwestern region of Indian subcontinent on both sides of the partition line ("<a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_Line" title="Radcliffe Line">Radcliffe Line</a>"). According to Banga and other scholars, the Sikhs had strongly opposed the <a href="/wiki/All-India_Muslim_League" title="All-India Muslim League">Muslim League</a> demands and saw it as "perpetuation of Muslim domination" and anti-Sikh policies in what just a hundred years before was a part of the Sikh Empire. As such, Sikh organizations, including the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Khalsa_Diwan" title="Chief Khalsa Diwan">Chief Khalsa Dewan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shiromani_Akali_Dal" title="Shiromani Akali Dal">Shiromani Akali Dal</a> led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Master_Tara_Singh" title="Master Tara Singh">Master Tara Singh</a>, condemned the <a href="/wiki/Lahore_Resolution" title="Lahore Resolution">Lahore Resolution</a> and the movement to create Pakistan, viewing it as inviting possible persecution; the Sikhs largely thus <a href="/wiki/Opposition_to_the_partition_of_India" title="Opposition to the partition of India">strongly opposed the partition of India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KudaisyaYong2004_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-KudaisyaYong2004-272">[262]</a></sup> During the discussions with the colonial authorities, Tara Singh emerged as an important leader who campaigned to prevent the partition of colonial India and for the recognition of Sikhs as a third community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_270-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100-270">[260]</a></sup>
</p><p>When partition was announced, the newly created line divided the Sikh population. Along with Hindus, Sikhs suffered organized violence and riots against them in West Pakistan. As a result, Sikhs moved en masse to the Indian side, leaving behind their property and holy sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abid2014_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abid2014-273">[263]</a></sup> However, the anti-Sikh violence was not one-sided. As Sikhs moved to the eastern side of the partition line, they engaged in reprisals against Muslims there, forcing them into Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_270-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100-270">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mann2014p81_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mann2014p81-274">[264]</a></sup> Before the partition, Sikhs constituted about 15% of the population in West Punjab, the majority being Muslims (55%). The Sikhs were the economic elite in West Punjab, however. They had the largest representation in West Punjab's aristocracy, and there were nearly 700 Gurdwaras and 400 educational institutions that served the interests of the Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–103_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–103-275">[265]</a></sup> Prior to the partition, there were a series of disputes between the majority Muslims and minority Sikhs, such as on the matters of <a href="/wiki/Jhatka" title="Jhatka">jhatka</a> versus <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a> meat, the disputed ownership of <a href="/wiki/Shaheed_Ganj_Mosque" title="Shaheed Ganj Mosque">Gurdwara Sahidganj</a> in Lahore which Muslims sought as a mosque and Sikhs as a Gurdwara, and the insistence of the provincial Muslim government on switching from Indian <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhi</a> script to Arabic-Persian <a href="/wiki/Nastaliq" title="Nastaliq">Nastaliq</a> script in schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_270-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100-270">[260]</a></sup> During and after the <a href="/wiki/Simla_Conference" title="Simla Conference">Simla Conference</a> in June 1945, headed by Lord Wavell, the Sikh leaders initially expressed their desire to be recognized as a third community, but ultimately relegated these demands and sought a United India where Sikhs, Hindus and Muslims would live together, under a Swiss-style constitution. The Muslim League rejected this approach, demanding that the entire Punjab should be granted to Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–104_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–104-276">[266]</a></sup> The Sikh leaders then sought the original partition instead, and the Congress Working Committee passed a resolution in support of partitioning Punjab and Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–104_276-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–104-276">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mann2014p81_274-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mann2014p81-274">[264]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2187" data-file-width="3268" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg/220px-Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg/330px-Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg/440px-Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sikh_Light_Infantry" title="Sikh Light Infantry">Sikh Light Infantry</a> personnel march past during the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Republic_Day_parade" title="Delhi Republic Day parade">Republic day parade</a> in New Delhi, India</div></div></div>
<p>Between March and August 1947, a series of riots, arson, plunder of Sikh and property, assassination of Sikh leaders, and killings in Jhelum districts, Rawalpindi, Attock and other places led to Tara Singh calling the situation in Punjab a "civil war", while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Mountbatten" title="Lord Mountbatten">Lord Mountbatten</a> stated "civil war preparations were going on".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abid2014_273-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abid2014-273">[263]</a></sup> The riots had triggered the early waves of migration in April, with some 20,000 people leaving northwest Punjab and moving to Patiala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga2017104–105_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga2017104–105-277">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shani2007p86_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shani2007p86-271">[261]</a></sup> In Rawalpindi, 40,000 people became homeless. The Sikh leaders made desperate petitions, but all religious communities were suffering in the political turmoil. Sikhs, states Banga, were "only 4 million out of a total of 28 million in Punjab, and 6 million out of nearly 400 million in India; they did not constitute the majority, not even in a single district".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga2017104–105_277-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga2017104–105-277">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolpert2010p9_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolpert2010p9-278">[268]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the partition line was formally announced in August 1947, the violence was unprecedented, with Sikhs being one of the most affected religious community both in terms of deaths, as well as property loss, injury, trauma and disruption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111-279">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mann2014p81_274-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mann2014p81-274">[264]</a></sup> Sikhs and Muslims were both victims and perpetrators of retaliatory violence against each other. Estimates range between 200,000 and 2 million deaths of Sikhs, Hindus and Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111_279-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111-279">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mann2014p81_274-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mann2014p81-274">[264]</a></sup> There were numerous rapes of and mass suicides by Sikh women, they being taken captives, their rescues and above all a mass exodus of Sikhs from newly created Pakistan into newly independent India. The partition created the "largest foot convoy of refugees recorded in [human] history, stretching over 100 kilometer long", states Banga, with nearly 300,000 people consisting of mostly "distraught, suffering, injured and angry Sikhs". Sikh and Hindu refugees from Pakistan flooded into India, Muslim refugees from India flooded into Pakistan, each into their new homeland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111_279-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111-279">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolpert2010p9_278-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wolpert2010p9-278">[268]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Khalistan">Khalistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Khalistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="456" data-file-width="608" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg/220px-Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg/330px-Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg/440px-Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sikhs in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> protesting against the Indian government</div></div></div>
<p>In 1940, a few Sikhs such as the victims of <a href="/wiki/Komagata_Maru_incident" title="Komagata Maru incident">Komagata Maru in Canada</a> proposed the idea of Khalistan as a buffer state between an independent India and what would become Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–103_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–103-275">[265]</a></sup> These leaders, however, were largely ignored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_270-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100-270">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shani2007p86_271-2"><a href="#cite_note-Shani2007p86-271">[261]</a></sup> The early 1980s witnessed some Sikh groups seeking an independent nation named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khalistan" title="Khalistan">Khalistan</a> carved out from India and Pakistan. The <a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple" title="Golden Temple">Golden Temple</a> and Akal Takht were occupied by various militant groups in 1982. These included the <a href="/wiki/Dharam_Yudh_Morcha" title="Dharam Yudh Morcha">Dharam Yudh Morcha</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Jarnail_Singh_Bhindranwale" title="Jarnail Singh Bhindranwale">Jarnail Singh Bhindranwale</a>, the Babbar Khalsa, the AISSF and the National Council of Khalistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chima2008p85_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chima2008p85-280">[270]</a></sup> Between 1982 and 1983, there were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anandpur_Resolution" title="Anandpur Resolution">Anandpur Resolution</a> demand-related terrorist attacks against civilians in parts of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horowitz482_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-horowitz482-281">[271]</a></sup> By late 1983, the Bhindranwale led group had begun to build bunkers and observations posts in and around the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple" title="Golden Temple">Golden Temple</a>, with militants involved in weapons training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chima2008p85_280-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chima2008p85-280">[270]</a></sup> In June 1984, the then <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_India" title="Prime Minister of India">Prime Minister of India</a> <a href="/wiki/Indira_Gandhi" title="Indira Gandhi">Indira Gandhi</a> ordered Indian Army to begin <a href="/wiki/Operation_Blue_Star" title="Operation Blue Star">Operation Blue Star</a> against the militants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chima2008p85_280-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chima2008p85-280">[270]</a></sup> The fierce engagement took place in the precincts of Darbar Sahib and resulted in many deaths, including Bhindranwale, the destruction of the Sikh Reference Library, which was considered a national treasure that contained over a thousand rare manuscripts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mann_114_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-mann_114-282">[272]</a></sup> and destroyed Akal Takht. Numerous soldiers, civilians and militants died in the cross fire. Within days of the Operation Bluestar, some 2,000 Sikh soldiers in India mutinied and attempted to reach Amritsar to liberate the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple" title="Golden Temple">Golden Temple</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chima2008p85_280-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chima2008p85-280">[270]</a></sup> Within six months, on 31 October 1984, Indira Gandhi's Sikh bodyguards <a href="/wiki/Satwant_Singh" title="Satwant Singh">Satwant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beant_Singh_(assassin)" title="Beant Singh (assassin)">Beant Singh</a> <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Indira_Gandhi" title="Assassination of Indira Gandhi">assassinated her</a>. The assassination triggered the <a href="/wiki/1984_anti-Sikh_riots" title="1984 anti-Sikh riots">1984 anti-Sikh riots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horowitz482_281-1"><a href="#cite_note-horowitz482-281">[271]</a></sup> According to Donald Horowitz, while anti-Sikh riots led to much damage and deaths, many serious provocations by militants also failed to trigger ethnic violence in many cases throughout the 1980s. The Sikhs and their neighbors, for most part, ignored attempts to provoke riots and communal strife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horowitz482_281-2"><a href="#cite_note-horowitz482-281">[271]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikh_people">Sikh people</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Sikh people">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikh" title="Sikh">Sikh</a></div>
<table class="sortable wikitable floatright">

<p>Estimates state that Sikhism has some 25-30 million followers worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[274]</a></sup> According to Pew Research, a religion demographics and research group in Washington DC, "more than nine-in-ten Sikhs are in India, but there are also sizable Sikh communities in the United States, the United Kingdom and Canada."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[275]</a></sup> Within India, the Sikh population is found in every state and union territory, but it is predominantly found in the northwestern and northern states. Only in the state of Punjab do Sikhs constitute a majority (58% of the total, per 2011 census).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census2011sikhs_283-1"><a href="#cite_note-census2011sikhs-283">[273]</a></sup> The states and union territories of India where Sikhs constitute more than 1.5% of its population are Punjab, Chandigarh, Haryana, Delhi, Uttarakhand and Jammu &amp; Kashmir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census2011sikhs_283-2"><a href="#cite_note-census2011sikhs-283">[273]</a></sup> Forming 4.7% of the total population, the western Canadian province of <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> is home to over 200,000 Sikhs and is the only province (or similar major subnational division) in the world outside India with Sikhism as the second most followed religion among the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[276]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[277]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sikhism was founded in northwestern region of the Indian subcontinent in what is now Pakistan. Some of the Gurus were born near <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> and in other parts of Pakistan. Prior to 1947, in British India, millions of Sikhs lived in what later became Pakistan. During the partition, Sikhs and Hindus left the newly created Muslim-majority Pakistan and mostly moved to Hindu-majority India — with some moving to Muslim-majority <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[278]</a></sup>) — while numerous Muslims in India moved to Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[279]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[280]</a></sup> According to 2017 news reports, only about 20,000 Sikhs remain in Pakistan, and their population is dwindling (<span class="nowrap">0.01%</span> of the country's estimated 200 million population).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[281]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[282]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikh_sects">Sikh sects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Sikh sects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sects_of_Sikhism" title="Sects of Sikhism">Sects of Sikhism</a></div>
<p>Sikh sects are sub-traditions within Sikhism that believe in an alternate lineage of gurus, or have a different interpretation of the Sikh scriptures, or believe in following a living guru, or hold other concepts that differ from the orthodox Khalsa Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Syan2014p170_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-Syan2014p170-293">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takhar2014p350_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-Takhar2014p350-294">[284]</a></sup> The major historic sects of Sikhism have included Udasi, Nirmala, Nanakpanthi, Khalsa, Sahajdhari, Namdhari Kuka, Nirankari, and Sarvaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994p24_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994p24-295">[285]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ustad_Baljit_Singh_Luxmi_Namdhari_and_Fateh_Singh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ustad_Baljit_Singh_Luxmi_Namdhari_and_Fateh_Singh.jpg/220px-Ustad_Baljit_Singh_Luxmi_Namdhari_and_Fateh_Singh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ustad_Baljit_Singh_Luxmi_Namdhari_and_Fateh_Singh.jpg/330px-Ustad_Baljit_Singh_Luxmi_Namdhari_and_Fateh_Singh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ustad_Baljit_Singh_Luxmi_Namdhari_and_Fateh_Singh.jpg/440px-Ustad_Baljit_Singh_Luxmi_Namdhari_and_Fateh_Singh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ustad_Baljit_Singh_Luxmi_Namdhari_and_Fateh_Singh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Namdhari Sikhs, also called the <i>Kuka</i> Sikhs are a sect of Sikhism known for their crisp white dress and horizontal <i>pagari</i> (turban).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britsikhssects_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-britsikhssects-296">[286]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaarKalsi2009p10_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-HaarKalsi2009p10-178">[172]</a></sup> Above: Namdhari singer and musicians.</div></div></div>
<p>The early Sikh sects were <a href="/wiki/Udasi" title="Udasi">Udasis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mina_(Sikhism)" title="Mina (Sikhism)">Minas</a> founded by <a href="/wiki/Sri_Chand" title="Sri Chand">Sri Chand</a> – the elder son of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mina_(Sikhism)" title="Mina (Sikhism)">Prithi Chand</a> – the elder son of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Ram_Das" title="Guru Ram Das">Guru Ram Das</a> respectively, in parallel to the official succession of the Sikh Gurus. Later on <a href="/wiki/Ramraiya" title="Ramraiya">Ramraiya</a> sect grew in <a href="/wiki/Dehradun" title="Dehradun">Dehradun</a> with the patronage of Aurangzeb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FenechMcLeod2014p260_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-FenechMcLeod2014p260-297">[287]</a></sup> Many splintered Sikh communities formed during the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> era. Some of these sects were financially and administratively supported by the Mughal rulers in the hopes of gaining a more favorable and compliant citizenry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takhar2014p350_294-1"><a href="#cite_note-Takhar2014p350-294">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FenechMcLeod2014p260_297-1"><a href="#cite_note-FenechMcLeod2014p260-297">[287]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the collapse of Mughal Empire, and particularly during the rule of Ranjit Singh, Udasi Sikhs protected Sikh shrines, preserved the Sikh scripture and rebuilt those that were desecrated or destroyed during the Muslim–Sikh wars. However, Udasi Sikhs kept idols and images inside these Sikh temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994p78_212-2"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994p78-212">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenech2014p375_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fenech2014p375-298">[288]</a></sup> In the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Namdhari" title="Namdhari">Namdharis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nirankari" title="Nirankari">Nirankaris</a> sects were formed in Sikhism, seeking to reform and return to what each believed was the pure form of Sikhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenech273_262-4"><a href="#cite_note-fenech273-262">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p28_260-3"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p28-260">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandair_2013_85–86_261-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair_2013_85–86-261">[251]</a></sup>
</p><p>All these sects differ from Khalsa orthodox Sikhs in their beliefs and practices, such as continuing to solemnize their weddings around fire and being strictly vegetarian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britsikhssects_296-1"><a href="#cite_note-britsikhssects-296">[286]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaarKalsi2009p10_178-2"><a href="#cite_note-HaarKalsi2009p10-178">[172]</a></sup> Many accept the concept of living Gurus such as Guru <a href="/wiki/Baba_Dyal_Singh" title="Baba Dyal Singh">Baba Dyal Singh</a>. The Nirankari sect, though unorthodox, was influential in shaping the views of Tat Khalsa and the contemporary-era Sikh beliefs and practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britsikhsects_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-britsikhsects-299">[289]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[290]</a></sup> Another significant Sikh sect of the 19th century was the Radhasoami movement in Punjab led by Baba Shiv Dyal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaarKalsi2009p9_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-HaarKalsi2009p9-301">[291]</a></sup> Other contemporary era Sikhs sects include the <a href="/wiki/Crypto-Hinduism" title="Crypto-Hinduism">quasi Hindu</a> <a href="/wiki/3HO" title="3HO">3HO</a>, formed in 1971, which exists outside India, particularly in North America and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaarKalsi2009p9_301-1"><a href="#cite_note-HaarKalsi2009p9-301">[291]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verne560_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-verne560-302">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[293]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikh_castes">Sikh castes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Sikh castes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nagar_Kirtan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1656" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Nagar_Kirtan.jpg/220px-Nagar_Kirtan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Nagar_Kirtan.jpg/330px-Nagar_Kirtan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Nagar_Kirtan.jpg/440px-Nagar_Kirtan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nagar_Kirtan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nagar_Kirtan" title="Nagar Kirtan">Nagar Kirtan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bangalore" title="Bangalore">Bangalore</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to Surinder Jodhka, the state of Punjab with a Sikh majority has the "largest proportion of <a href="/wiki/Dalit" title="Dalit">scheduled caste</a> population in India". Although decried by Sikhism, Sikhs have practiced a caste system. The system, along with untouchability, has been more common in rural parts of Punjab. The landowning dominant Sikh castes, states Jodhka, "have not shed all their prejudices against the lower castes or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dalits" title="Dalits">dalits</a>; while dalits would be allowed entry into the village gurdwaras they would not be permitted to cook or serve langar." The Sikh dalits of Punjab have tried to build their own gurdwara, other local level institutions and sought better material circumstances and dignity. According to Jodhka, due to economic mobility in contemporary Punjab, castes no longer mean an inherited occupation, nor are work relations tied to a single location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[294]</a></sup>
In 1953, the government of India acceded to the demands of the Sikh leader, <a href="/wiki/Tara_Singh_(activist)" title="Tara Singh (activist)">Master Tara Singh</a>, to include Sikh Dalit castes in the list of scheduled castes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harish_K._Puri_2004_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harish_K._Puri_2004-305">[295]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiromani_Gurdwara_Prabandhak_Committee" title="Shiromani Gurdwara Prabandhak Committee">Shiromani Gurdwara Prabandhak Committee</a>, 20 of the 140 seats are reserved for low-caste Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harish_K._Puri_2004_305-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harish_K._Puri_2004-305">[295]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over 60% of Sikhs belong to the <a href="/wiki/Jat_Sikh" title="Jat Sikh">Jat</a> caste, which is an agrarian caste. Despite being very small in numbers, the mercantile <a href="/wiki/Khatri" title="Khatri">Khatri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arora" title="Arora">Arora</a> castes wield considerable influence within the Sikh community. Other common Sikh castes include <a href="/wiki/Saini" title="Saini">Sainis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ramgarhia" title="Ramgarhia">Ramgarhias</a> (artisans), <a href="/wiki/Ahluwalia_(caste)" title="Ahluwalia (caste)">Ahluwalias</a> (formerly brewers), <a href="/wiki/Rai_Sikh" title="Rai Sikh">Rai sikh</a> (Raa), <a href="/wiki/Kamboj" title="Kamboj">Kambojs</a> (rural caste), <a href="/wiki/Labana" title="Labana">Labanas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kumhar" title="Kumhar">Kumhars</a> and the two Dalit castes, known in Sikh terminology as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazhabi" title="Mazhabi">Mazhabis</a> (the Chuhras) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ravidasia" title="Ravidasia">Ravidasias</a> (the Chamars).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[296]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikh_diaspora">Sikh diaspora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Sikh diaspora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sikhism_in_India" title="Sikhism in India">Sikhism in India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikh_diaspora" title="Sikh diaspora">Sikh diaspora</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sikhism_by_country" title="Sikhism by country">Sikhism by country</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sikhs_on_the_move!.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sikhs_on_the_move%21.jpg/220px-Sikhs_on_the_move%21.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sikhs_on_the_move%21.jpg/330px-Sikhs_on_the_move%21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sikhs_on_the_move%21.jpg/440px-Sikhs_on_the_move%21.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sikhs_on_the_move!.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sikhs celebrating <a href="/wiki/Vaisakhi" title="Vaisakhi">Vaisakhi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, Canada</div></div></div>
<p>Sikhism is <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups#Religious_demographics" title="Major religious groups">the fourth-largest amongst the medium-sized world religions</a>, and one of the youngest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partridge2013_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-Partridge2013-307">[297]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDowellBrown2009_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-McDowellBrown2009-308">[298]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teece2005_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-Teece2005-309">[299]</a></sup> Worldwide, there are 30 million Sikhs, which makes up 0.4% of the world's population. Approximately 75% of Sikhs live in <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab</a>, where they constitute over 58% of the state's population. Large communities of Sikhs migrate to the neighboring states such as Indian State of Haryana which is home to the second largest Sikh population in India with 1.1 million Sikhs as per 2001 census, and large immigrant communities of Sikhs can be found across India. However, Sikhs only comprise about 2% of the Indian population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh_Kalsi_2007_12_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh_Kalsi_2007_12-310">[300]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sikh migration to Canada began in the 19th century and led to the creation of significant Sikh communities, predominantly in South <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surrey,_British_Columbia" title="Surrey, British Columbia">Surrey, British Columbia</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brampton,_Ontario" title="Brampton, Ontario">Brampton, Ontario</a>. Today temples, newspapers, radio stations, and markets cater to these large, multi-generational <a href="/wiki/Indo-Canadians" title="Indo-Canadians">Indo-Canadian</a> groups. Sikh festivals such as Vaisakhi and <a href="/wiki/Bandi_Chhor_Divas" title="Bandi Chhor Divas">Bandi Chhor</a> are celebrated in those Canadian cities by the largest groups of followers in the world outside the Punjab.
</p><p>Sikhs also migrated to East Africa, West Africa, the Middle East, Southeast Asia, the United Kingdom, the United States, and Australia. These communities developed as Sikhs migrated out of Punjab to fill in gaps in imperial labour markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[301]</a></sup> In the early twentieth century a significant community began to take shape on the west coast of the United States. Smaller populations of Sikhs are found within many countries in Western Europe, Pakistan, Mauritius, Malaysia, Philippines, Fiji, Nepal, China, Afghanistan, Iran, Singapore, United States, and many other countries.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prohibitions_in_Sikhism">Prohibitions in Sikhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Prohibitions in Sikhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Prohibitions_in_Sikhism" title="Prohibitions in Sikhism">Prohibitions in Sikhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diet_in_Sikhism" title="Diet in Sikhism">Diet in Sikhism</a></div>
<p>These prohibitions are strictly followed by initiated <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a> Sikhs who have undergone baptism. While the Sikh gurus did not enforce religion and did not believe in forcing people to follow any particular religion in general, the Sikh community does encourage all people to become better individuals by following the Guru's Way (<a href="/wiki/Gurmat" title="Gurmat">Gur-mat</a>), as opposed to living life without the Guru's code of disciple (Man-mat):
</p><p>4 major transgressions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[302]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Hair removal – <a href="/wiki/Hair" title="Hair">Hair</a> <a href="/wiki/Cutting" title="Cutting">cutting</a>, trimming, removing, <a href="/wiki/Shaving" title="Shaving">shaving</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plucking_(hair_removal)" title="Plucking (hair removal)">plucking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Threading_(epilation)" title="Threading (epilation)">threading</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyeing_hair" title="Dyeing hair">dyeing</a>, or any other alteration from any body part is strictly forbidden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[303]</a></sup></li>
<li>Eating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kutha_meat" title="Kutha meat">Kutha</a> meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[304]</a></sup> This is the absolute minimum required by all <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">initiated Sikhs</a>. Many Sikhs refrain from eating <a href="/wiki/Non-vegetarian" title="Non-vegetarian">non-vegetarian</a> food, and believe all should follow this diet. This is due to various social, cultural, political, and familial aspects. As such, there has always been major disagreement among Sikhs over the issue of eating <a href="/wiki/Non-vegetarian" title="Non-vegetarian">non-vegetarian</a> food. Sikhs following the rahit (code of conduct) of the <a href="/wiki/Damdami_Taksal" title="Damdami Taksal">Damdami Taksal</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Akhand_Kirtani_Jatha" title="Akhand Kirtani Jatha">AKJ</a> also subscribe to this view. The <a href="/wiki/Nihang" title="Nihang">Akali Nihangs</a> have traditionally eaten meat and are famous for performing <a href="/wiki/Jhatka" title="Jhatka">Jhatka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[305]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahaprashad_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahaprashad-316">[306]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sikh_Goat_Sacrifice_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sikh_Goat_Sacrifice-317">[307]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sikh_Goat_Sacrifice2_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sikh_Goat_Sacrifice2-318">[308]</a></sup> Thus, there is a wide range of views that exist on the issue of a proper "Sikh diet" in the <a href="/wiki/Panth" title="Panth">Panth</a>. Nonetheless, all Sikhs agree with the minimum consensus that meat slaughtered via the Muslim (<a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">Halal</a>) or Jewish (<a href="/wiki/Shechita" title="Shechita">Shechita</a>) methods is strictly against Sikh dogma and principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[310]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Akal_Takht" title="Akal Takht">Akal Takht</a> represents the final authority on controversial issues concerning the Sikh <a href="/wiki/Panth" title="Panth">Panth</a> (community or collective). The <a href="/wiki/Hukamnama" title="Hukamnama">Hukamnama</a> (edict or clarification), issued by <a href="/wiki/Akal_Takht" title="Akal Takht">Akal Takht</a> <a href="/wiki/Jathedar" title="Jathedar">Jathedar</a> <a href="/wiki/Jathedar_Sadhu_Singh_Bhaura" title="Jathedar Sadhu Singh Bhaura">Sadhu Singh Bhaura</a> dated 15 February 1980, states that eating meat does not go against the code of conduct of the Sikhs. <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Amritdhari</a> Sikhs can eat meat as long as it is <a href="/wiki/Jhatka" title="Jhatka">Jhatka</a> meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh_2001_89_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh_2001_89-321">[311]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">Adultery</a>: Cohabiting with a person other than one's spouse (sexual relations with anyone who you are not married to).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[312]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[313]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[314]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[315]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[316]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[317]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[318]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intoxications" title="Intoxications">Intoxication</a> – A Sikh must not take <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cannabis" title="Cannabis">cannabis</a>), <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquor" title="Liquor">liquor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a>, in short, any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intoxicant" title="Intoxicant">intoxicant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[319]</a></sup> Consumption of <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intoxicants" title="Intoxicants">intoxicants</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquor" title="Liquor">liquor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narcotic" title="Narcotic">narcotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a>, etc.) is not allowed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[320]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacauliffe1909[[s:Page:The_Sikh_Religion,_its_gurus,_sacred_writings_and_authors_Vol_1.djvu/27|xxi]]_331-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacauliffe1909[[s:Page:The_Sikh_Religion,_its_gurus,_sacred_writings_and_authors_Vol_1.djvu/27|xxi]]-331">[321]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_and_Sikhism" title="Cannabis and Sikhism">Cannabis is generally prohibited</a>, but ritually consumed in edible form by some Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014-332">[322]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinghHawley2012_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-SinghHawley2012-333">[323]</a></sup> Some Sikh groups, like the <a href="/wiki/Damdami_Taksal" title="Damdami Taksal">Damdami Taksal</a>, are even opposed to drinking <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">Indian tea</a>. Indian tea is almost always served in Sikh <a href="/wiki/Gurdwara" title="Gurdwara">Gurudwaras</a> around the world. Some <a href="/wiki/Nihang" title="Nihang">Akali Nihang</a> groups consume <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis-containing</a> shaheedi degh (<span title="Punjabi-language text"><span lang="pa">ਭੰਗ</span></span>), purportedly to help in <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[324]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[325]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[326]</a></sup><i>Sūkha parshaad</i> (<span title="Punjabi-language text"><span lang="pa">ਸੁੱਖਾ ਪ੍ਰਰਸਾਦ</span></span>), "Dry-sweet", is the term <a href="/wiki/Nihang" title="Nihang">Akali Nihangs</a> use to refer to it. It was traditionally crushed and consumed as a liquid, especially during festivals like <a href="/wiki/Hola_Mohalla" title="Hola Mohalla">Hola Mohalla</a>. It is never smoked, as this practice is forbidden in Sikhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[327]</a></sup> In 2001, <a href="/wiki/Jathedar_Santa_Singh" title="Jathedar Santa Singh">Jathedar Santa Singh</a>, the leader of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Budha_Dal" title="Budha Dal">Budha Dal</a>, along with 20 chiefs of <a href="/wiki/Nihang" title="Nihang">Nihang</a> sects, refused to accept the ban on consumption of <i> shaheedi degh</i> by the apex Sikh clergy of <a href="/wiki/Akal_Takht" title="Akal Takht">Akal Takht</a> - in order to preserve their traditional practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[328]</a></sup> According to a recent BBC article, "Traditionally they also drank shaheedi degh, an infusion of cannabis, to become closer with God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[329]</a></sup> Baba Santa Singh was excommunicated and replaced with Baba Balbir Singh, who agreed to shun the consumption of <i>bhang</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[330]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Other mentioned practices to be avoided, as per the Sikh Rehat Maryada:
</p>
<ul><li>Piercing of the nose or ears for wearing ornaments is forbidden for Sikh men and women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[331]</a></sup></li>
<li>Female infanticide: A Sikh should not kill his daughter; nor should he maintain any relationship with a killer of daughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[332]</a></sup></li>
<li>A Sikh shall not <a href="/wiki/Theft" title="Theft">steal</a>, form dubious associations or engage in <a href="/wiki/Gambling" title="Gambling">gambling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[333]</a></sup></li>
<li>It is not proper for a Sikh <a href="/wiki/Woman" title="Woman">woman</a> to wear <a href="/wiki/Veil" title="Veil">veil</a> or keep her <a href="/wiki/Face" title="Face">face</a> hidden by <a href="/wiki/Veil" title="Veil">veil</a> or cover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[334]</a></sup></li>
<li>Sikhs cannot wear any token of any other faith. Sikhs must not have their head bare or wear <a href="/wiki/Cap" title="Cap">caps</a>. They also cannot wear any <a href="/wiki/Ornament_(art)" title="Ornament (art)">ornaments</a> piercing through any part of the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[335]</a></sup></li>
<li>Hereditary priest – Sikhism does not have priests, as they were abolished by Guru Gobind Singh (the 10th Guru of Sikhism).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-346">[336]</a></sup> The only position he left was a <a href="/wiki/Granthi" title="Granthi">Granthi</a> to look after the Guru Granth Sahib; any Sikh is free to become Granthi or read from the Guru Granth Sahib.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_346-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-346">[336]</a></sup></li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35-198"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35_198-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35_198-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"Technically this would place the Sikh community's origins at a much further remove than 1469, perhaps to the dawning of the Sant movement, which possesses clear affinities to Guru Nanak's thought sometime in the tenth century. </li>
<li id="cite_note-David_Lorenzen_1995_pages_1-2-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-David_Lorenzen_1995_pages_1-2_200-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Historically, Sikh religion derives from this nirguni current of bhakti religion." (McLeod 2019/1998).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-204"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-204">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These views include Sikhs believing in achieving blissful mukhti while alive, Sikhs placing emphasis on the path of the householder, Sikhs disbelief in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahinsa" title="Ahinsa">Ahinsa</a>, and the Sikhs afterlife aspect of merging with God rather than a physical heaven.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some disagree with this viewpoint, and state that <i>guru</i> in Sikhism is "not a teacher or a guide", but "God's own manifestation"; see: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFSinghSingh2002">Singh, Bhagat; Singh, G. P. (2002). <i>Japji</i>. Hemkunt Press. p. 9. <q>In Sikh religion the word 'Guru' does not denote a teacher, or an expert or a guide in human body. When God manifested his attributes in person, that person was called 'Guru Nanak'</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Japji&amp;rft.pages=9&amp;rft.pub=Hemkunt+Press&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.aulast=Singh&amp;rft.aufirst=Bhagat&amp;rft.au=Singh%2C+G.+P.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASikhism"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMann2001">Mann, Gurinder Singh (2001). <i>The Making of Sikh Scripture</i>. <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">Oxford University Press</a>. p. 19. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-513024-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-513024-9"><bdi>978-0-19-513024-9</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Making+of+Sikh+Scripture&amp;rft.pages=19&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-513024-9&amp;rft.aulast=Mann&amp;rft.aufirst=Gurinder+Singh&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASikhism"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFEraly2015">Eraly, Abraham (2015). <i>The Age of Wrath: A History of the Delhi Sultanate</i>. Penguin UK. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-93-5118-658-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-93-5118-658-8"><bdi>978-93-5118-658-8</bdi></a>. <q>The main thrust of Kabir's mission was to unite Hindus and Muslims in a common quest for god realisation. 'Hindus and Muslims have the same god,, he held. 'God is the breath of all breath ... Look within your heart, for there you will find [God] ... All men and women in the world are his living forms.' Although many of his sayings had a strong Hindu flavour in them—presumably because of Ramananda's influence—he made no distinction between Hinduism and Islam Similarly, though he usually referred to god as Hari or Rama, he used those words as synonyms of god, and not as the names of particular deities. 'I am not Hindu nor Muslim; Allah-Ram is the breath of my body,' he stated, and went on to declare that All that lives and dies, they are all one. The this and that haggling, is done.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Age+of+Wrath%3A+A+History+of+the+Delhi+Sultanate&amp;rft.pub=Penguin+UK&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft.isbn=978-93-5118-658-8&amp;rft.aulast=Eraly&amp;rft.aufirst=Abraham&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASikhism"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-134">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFSusanne2013">Susanne, Scholz (2013). <i>God Loves Diversity and Justice</i>. Lexington Books. p. 16. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7391-7318-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7391-7318-3"><bdi>978-0-7391-7318-3</bdi></a>. <q>The Sikh Divine comprises every imaginable theological ideal! Hindu, Buddhist, Tantric, and Islamic views that were current in medieval India come together in the wide-ranging literary spectrum of the GGS. The stereotypical oppositions between the Indic and Abrahamic worldviews of the day are transcended: "Some call it Rama, some call it Khuda; some worship it as Vishnu, some as Allah" (GGS: 885). Interestingly, even the atheistic Buddhist Nirvana is not omitted: "Itself Nirvana, It itself relishes pleasures" (GGS: 97). "God" or "gods" or "no god" alike are recognized as part of the infinite One! "Always, always you alone are the One Reality – <i>sada sada tun eku hai</i>" (GGS: 139). Persian terminology is used to emphasize the unity of being: "<i>asti ek digari kui ek tui ek tui</i> – Only the One is, there is none other; Only you, you only" (GGS: 144). Again, "<i>hindu turk ka sahib ek</i>  – Hindus and Muslims share the One sovereign" (GGS: 1158). (The term "Turk" referred to all Muslims in this period.) Since everything is a manifestation of That One being, all the manifestations would be a part of it. No god, no body, and no thing is excluded from this all pervasive being</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=God+Loves+Diversity+and+Justice&amp;rft.pages=16&amp;rft.pub=Lexington+Books&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7391-7318-3&amp;rft.aulast=Susanne&amp;rft.aufirst=Scholz&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASikhism"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-king359-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-king359_135-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Anna S. King and JL Brockington (2005), The Intimate Other: Love Divine in Indic Religions, Orient Blackswan, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-81-250-2801-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-81-250-2801-7">978-81-250-2801-7</a>, pp. 359–361</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-141">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cole, William Owen; Sambhi, Piara Singh (1995), <i>The Sikhs: Their Religious Beliefs and Practices</i>, Sussex Academic Press, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-898723-13-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-898723-13-4">978-1-898723-13-4</a>, p. 44</span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100-270"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_270-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_270-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_270-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_270-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_270-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBanga2017">Banga 2017</a>, pp. 99–100.</span>

<li id="cite_note-Shani2007p86-271"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Shani2007p86_271-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Shani2007p86_271-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Shani2007p86_271-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFShani2007">Shani, Giorgio (2007). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=HKu66SixH6AC" rel="nofollow"><i>Sikh Nationalism and Identity in a Global Age</i></a>. Abingdon-on-Thames, England: <a href="/wiki/Routledge" title="Routledge">Routledge</a>. pp. 86–93. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-134-10189-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-134-10189-4"><bdi>978-1-134-10189-4</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Sikh+Nationalism+and+Identity+in+a+Global+Age&amp;rft.place=Abingdon-on-Thames%2C+England&amp;rft.pages=86-93&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-134-10189-4&amp;rft.aulast=Shani&amp;rft.aufirst=Giorgio&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DHKu66SixH6AC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASikhism"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-KudaisyaYong2004-272"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-KudaisyaYong2004_272-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFKudaisyaYong2004">Kudaisya, Gyanesh; Yong, Tan Tai (2004). <i>The Aftermath of Partition in South Asia</i>. Abingdon-on-Thames, England: <a href="/wiki/Routledge" title="Routledge">Routledge</a>. p. 100. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-134-44048-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-134-44048-1"><bdi>978-1-134-44048-1</bdi></a>. <q>No sooner was it made public than the Sikhs launched a virulent campaign against the Lahore Resolution. Pakistan was portrayed as a possible return to an unhappy past when Sikhs were persecuted and Muslims the persecutor. Public speeches by various Sikh political leaders on the subject of Pakistan invariably invoked images of atrocities committed by Muslims against Sikhs and of the martyrdom of their <i>gurus</i> and heroes. Reactions to the Lahore Resolution were uniformly negative and Sikh leaders of all political persuasions made it clear that Pakistan would be 'wholeheartedly resisted'. The Shiromani Akali Dal, which had a substantial following among rural Sikhs, organized several well-attended conferences in Lahore to condemn the Muslim League. Master Tara Singh, leader of the Akali Dal, declared that his party would fight Pakistan 'tooth and nail'. Not be outdone, other Sikh political organizations, rival to the Akali Dal, namely the Central Khalsa Young Men Union and the moderate and loyalist Chief Khalsa Dewan, declared in equally strong language their unequivocal opposition to the Pakistan scheme.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Aftermath+of+Partition+in+South+Asia&amp;rft.place=Abingdon-on-Thames%2C+England&amp;rft.pages=100&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-134-44048-1&amp;rft.aulast=Kudaisya&amp;rft.aufirst=Gyanesh&amp;rft.au=Yong%2C+Tan+Tai&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASikhism"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Abid2014-273"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Abid2014_273-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Abid2014_273-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFAbid2014">Abid, Abdul Majeed (29 December 2014). <a class="external text" href="https://nation.com.pk/29-Dec-2014/the-forgotten-massacre" rel="nofollow">"The forgotten massacre"</a>. <i>The Nation</i>. <q>On the same dates, Muslim League-led mobs fell with determination and full preparations on the helpless Hindus and Sikhs scattered in the villages of Multan, Rawalpindi, Campbellpur, Jhelum and Sargodha. The murderous mobs were well supplied with arms, such as daggers, swords, spears and fire-arms. (A former civil servant mentioned in his autobiography that weapon supplies had been sent from NWFP and money was supplied by Delhi-based politicians.) They had bands of stabbers and their auxiliaries, who covered the assailant, ambushed the victim and if necessary disposed of his body. These bands were subsidized monetarily by the Muslim League, and cash payments were made to individual assassins based on the numbers of Hindus and Sikhs killed. There were also regular patrolling parties in jeeps which went about sniping and picking off any stray Hindu or Sikh. ... Thousands of non-combatants including women and children were killed or injured by mobs, supported by the All India Muslim League.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Nation&amp;rft.atitle=The+forgotten+massacre&amp;rft.date=2014-12-29&amp;rft.aulast=Abid&amp;rft.aufirst=Abdul+Majeed&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fnation.com.pk%2F29-Dec-2014%2Fthe-forgotten-massacre&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASikhism"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Mann2014p81-274"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Mann2014p81_274-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Mann2014p81_274-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Mann2014p81_274-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Mann2014p81_274-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMann2014">Mann, Michael (2014). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=TBscBQAAQBAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>South Asia's Modern History: Thematic Perspectives</i></a>. Abingdon-on-Thames, England: <a href="/wiki/Routledge" title="Routledge">Routledge</a>. pp. 81–83. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-317-62446-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-317-62446-2"><bdi>978-1-317-62446-2</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=South+Asia%27s+Modern+History%3A+Thematic+Perspectives&amp;rft.place=Abingdon-on-Thames%2C+England&amp;rft.pages=81-83&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-317-62446-2&amp;rft.aulast=Mann&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DTBscBQAAQBAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASikhism"></span></span>
